HEADLINE: Child Support Payments to Mothers Improved From '93 to '99 

BYLINE:  AP 

DATELINE: WASHINGTON, Oct. 26 

 BODY:
The number of mothers who received all the child support they were owed increased by more than 25 percent in the late 1990's, the Census Bureau has reported.
 A strong economy and stricter enforcement led more fathers to pay in full, bureau analysts said on Friday. 
 More than 2.8 million women collected all the child support owed to them, nearly 46 percent of the mothers entitled to payments in 1999. That was an increase from nearly 2.2 million, or almost 37 percent, of the mothers owed support in 1993. 
The report included other positive statistics for mothers raising children alone. More custodial mothers worked, fewer lived in poverty and fewer collected public assistance in the six years from 1993 to 1999. The 1996 welfare changes, which are up for renewal in Congress, nudged more single mothers off public assistance and into jobs.
Critics noted that the report contained no information about life for single parents since the economic decline started in 2000.
"The problem we are seeing now is that so many people are struggling again," said Geraldine Jensen, president of the Association for Children for Enforcement of Support. 
The portion of custodial mothers who received none of the child support they were owed remained relatively unchanged at 25 percent between 1993 and 1999.
Still, said David Siegal, deputy commissioner for child support enforcement for the Department of Health and Human Services, enforcement cases have increased since federal and state governments started cracking down.
The Census Bureau's survey, every two years, is the nation's only estimate of all child support paid and owed across the country, including amounts set in private agreements between parents. The Department of Health and Human Services keeps more recent figures, but only for cases that go through government collection systems.
Among custodial fathers, about 248,000 of 658,000, almost 38 percent, received the full amount of child support from absent mothers. More than 35 percent of custodial fathers in 1999 received none of the child support they were due, up from about 26 percent six years earlier, though the Census Bureau did not consider that change to be statistically significant.

HEADLINE: Summer's Storms Hurt Wine Industry in Italy 

BYLINE:  By The New York Times 

DATELINE: ROME, Oct. 26 

 BODY:
The floods that plagued Central Europe this summer also wreaked havoc on Italy's wine industry, ruining whole vineyards and hurting an important sector of the economy. 
 Constant rainstorms and bursts of hailstones battered vines across the country, causing one of the worst harvests in decades, with losses estimated at around $200 million. 
 "It seemed like the sky was falling," said Ernesto Abbona, who makes Marchesi di Barolo wine in Piedmont in the north. When it hailed in September, he said, he lost more than 40 percent of his vineyard. 
Mr. Abbona's ordeal is not isolated, as vintners across Europe suffered damage. Heavy rains soaked vineyards in southern Greece, scrapping the 2002 Nemea, one of the country's favorite red wines, while floodwaters postponed timely harvests in the Rhone Valley in France. 
Production in Italy, Europe's biggest winemaker after France, was hit especially hard, as bad weather reduced the wine yield to 10 percent below last year's, according to the agricultural research group Ismea. One effect may be to drive up export prices on the smaller harvest. 
Heavy rainfall in southern regions like Sicily allowed for grape-spoiling mold, while northern regions like Veneto had to contend with hail that chopped down vines. The combination proved costly for Italy. Mauro Alpagotti, director of the Italian Agriculture Confederation, said the damage had cost about $200 million.
Enzo Rivella, president of an association of Italian wine producers, said, "This is one of the most difficult harvests I've seen in 30 years, not only for the loss of production, but also for the damage to quality." 
Italian shoppers are just now starting to see the consequences of that poor harvest, as the prices of some table wines have shot up 10 percent, driven by the diminished supply and the need of producers to pay for uninsured losses. Lombardy, Pulia and Sicily all registered 20 percent drops in production, while weather damage in September to Piedmont, Veneto and other northern regions prompted the government to declare a state of natural disaster, supplying about $150 million in aid to those areas. 
The paltry harvest is also expected to increase the price of much of the wine sold in the United States.

HEADLINE: Hawaiians Find an Unlikely Eden in Las Vegas 

BYLINE:  By LAWRENCE DOWNES 

DATELINE: LAS VEGAS, Oct. 20 

 BODY:
Some mornings when the beach is empty and the waves are perfect, five-foot left curls crashing gently onto water smooth as glass, Moses Mokuahi goes surfing. He savors every second of the ride, kicking out just before the swell collapses into foam and trickles into drain holes at the edge of a low concrete wall.
 "The best thing about it, it's consistent," Mr. Mokuahi said. "Every 108 seconds you got that same wave. After I catch five, six waves, I turn the system off. It puts a smile on my face the rest of the day." 
 Mr. Mokuahi, 49, a former construction foreman who moved here from Oahu six years ago, considers himself one lucky Hawaiian, and not just because he controls the only surfable body of water on the Las Vegas Strip -- the turbine-driven one he helped build at the Mandalay Bay Resort and Casino. 
It is because he has a job -- wave-pool director -- that lets him live in a nice suburb, in a house big enough for him, his wife, their 19-year-old son and any other relatives who visit.
Never mind that Mr. Mokuahi left a rural Eden of red dirt, towering ironwood trees and the finest surfing anywhere to ride push-button waves inside a tourist-washing machine.
Las Vegas, he says, has given him material comfort he could never have attained back home. Mr. Mokuahi, a muscular, dark-haired man with a bristly mustache, misses Hawaii -- "the best place in the world to be," he says matter-of-factly. But he is not complaining.
Neither, it seems, are the tens of thousands of other Hawaiians who have joined him in this casino boomtown. A broad swath of the Hawaiian middle class -- hotel workers, retired police officers, teachers and, increasingly, nurses and other professionals -- abandoned its economically depressed home state in the last 10 years, seeking better jobs, cheaper houses and easier lives here.
This stream of migrants has merged with a deluge of visitors -- Hawaiians make 300,000 trips here every year -- to transform Las Vegas into perhaps the most Hawaiian city on the mainland.
Las Vegas has long been Hawaiians' No. 1 travel destination, a vacation Mecca's vacation Mecca, thanks to casino-sponsored charter packages that make crossing the Pacific cheaper than hopping to a neighbor island, and a well-known Hawaiian passion for gambling.
This virtual Aloha State has its own radio programs, Web sites and even casino hotels, where plainclothes security men wear aloha shirts, and restaurants and gift shops cater to obscure Hawaiian tastes and traditions, like Spam for breakfast and, bafflingly, beef jerky for the folks back home.
Las Vegas is so Hawaiian that island high schools have their reunions here -- 40 this year at the California Hotel and Casino alone. This 800-room hotel, known to Hawaiians as "the Cal," is one of a handful of hotels north of the Strip that cater almost exclusively to Hawaiians.
The irony of pilgrims streaming out of a tropical paradise into the desert, like the Rapture in reverse, was not lost on many expatriates interviewed here.
But they said the harsh facts of island life -- high unemployment and taxes, a slumping tourist economy, staggering housing costs -- justified the move. Couples can hold three jobs, many said, and never afford a house in Hawaii, where the median house price is $345,000. Nevada's median is $152,000.
"I'd be in a shack with a tin roof back home," said Stephanie Moldenhauer, a cabdriver who came here seven years ago. She and her husband, Bobby, own a 1,500-square-foot house in suburban Henderson. "The house we have here would be half a million on Kauai," she said.
Debbie Shinogi, a nurse, and her husband, Tracy, a casino employee, own a three-bedroom home in Henderson. Back in Aiea, they lived with Mr. Shinogi's parents.
"Here you can get 18 eggs for 79 cents," Ms. Shinogi said. "Back home it's two bucks or something. Cereal is $5 to $6 a box in Hawaii. Here you can get two boxes for $5. We eat out a lot, because it's so much cheaper to eat out than to cook for two people."
But Robert Parker, a sociology professor at the University of Nevada, Las Vegas who has written about the social costs of the southern Nevada boom, said people should consider the downside of moving here.
He ticked off a litany of Las Vegas pathologies: the nation's highest suicide rates among teenagers and the elderly; the highest per capita pedestrian fatality rate; severe air pollution; rampant asthma. 
Factor in all the bad things, he said, and there is only one honest conclusion. "This is not an affordable place to live." 
Still, the outsiders -- and not just Hawaiians -- keep coming. This city's population has exploded by nearly 86 percent since 1990, according to the census. It welcomed 35 million visitors last year. 
Las Vegas is not the only way station for the Hawaiian diaspora. California and the Pacific Northwest have lured islanders for generations. But only Las Vegas has Ohana, a magazine for Hawaiian expatriates, a group estimated at 55,000 to 65,000. Only Las Vegas has at least four stores serving Hawaiians' beef jerky needs. Only Las Vegas has the Hawaiian gambling throngs and all those high school reunions.
The Las Vegas Convention and Visitors Authority says tiny Hawaii, the 42nd most populous state with 1.2 million residents, ranked 12th in air traffic to Las Vegas last year.
This is why Mr. Mokuahi, 2,700 miles from his hometown, spends his days surrounded by Hawaiians.
There are the lifeguards at the wave pool: Lew from Waianae, Brandon from Hilo, Jonathan from Kaneohe, Larry from Kailua and about a half-dozen others.
Then there are the people he meets in his off hours at the Cal, where the corridors ring with the mellow tones of pidgin English and elderly gamblers maneuver oxygen tanks and motorized wheelchairs among rows of blinking, bleeping slot machines.
Don Inamine, a retired schoolteacher from Oahu, is a typical Cal denizen. "I come for golf two times a year," said Mr. Inamine, 67, sitting at a lunch counter with his wife, Arlene. The other two to three yearly visits, he said, are strictly for gambling -- about four hours a day, and never more than $2,000 to $3,000 on a trip.
Repeat visitors like Mr. Inamine make life more bearable for those who have stayed. Debbie Shinogi said she and her husband play host to Hawaiians practically every month.
The Hawaiian Airlines flight one Saturday morning brought Gloria Kaauwai, Ms. Moldenhauer's mother, to McCarran International Airport. She had a large plastic ice chest sealed with duct tape.
Ms. Moldenhauer's eyes gleamed when she explained what she thought was inside. "Opihi, raw fish, laulau," she said -- perishable luau staples.
Her family visits once a year and she goes back once a year, but it is never enough.
"I wish I was on the beach," Ms. Moldenhauer said. She thinks of the friends she loves and misses. "I have videotapes of us hanging out at the beach. I have my Hawaii calendar I can look at." Her eyes brimmed with tears.
She was not the only one to muse about the cruelty of trading an organic paradise for a synthetic one.
"Eating papayas and drinking guava juice on the beach -- how do you top that? You can't," said Stephen Lum, whose Las Vegas real estate business, No Ka Oi Realty, has handled hundreds of Hawaiian relocations. "In Hawaii you have a better quality of life. The air and water are cleaner. In Vegas you have a better standard of living."

HEADLINE: THREATS AND RESPONSES: SEARCH FOR FUGITIVES; 
Yemen Turns to Tribes to Aid Hunt for Qaeda

BYLINE:  By NEIL MacFARQUHAR 

DATELINE: MARIB, Yemen, Oct. 23 

 BODY:
Given the penchant of tribesmen in this isolated province to tote around weapons like personal bazookas, the Yemeni government is holding hostage about 40 sons of tribal leaders. 
 The sons -- between the ages of 15 and 20 -- have been detained in military prison for nine months to ensure Yemeni troops a modicum of peace while they search for fugitive members of Al Qaeda. The government believes that as long as the young men remain captive 100 miles away in Sana, the capital, any tribesman inclined to see its troops as interlopers or American stooges will think twice before opening fire. 
 "It is something ordinary in Yemen, a tradition," said Sheik Ahmed Ali bin Jallal, the leader of the 140,000-strong Abida tribe, who wears a curved dagger and a Nokia cellphone in his heavily embroidered belt. "We do it just to prove our loyalty to the president." 
President Ali Abdullah Saleh demands tribal loyalty in this particular case to fulfill his own pledge to Washington of complete cooperation in the fight against terrorism. It is expected to be a protracted struggle in a country whose traditions include fractious tribes, a certain proclivity for radical Islam and the kind of widespread poverty that can help breed extremism. 
Yemen proved a somewhat reluctant partner with the United States while investigating the suicide mission two years ago against the destroyer Cole, which killed 17 American sailors and was eventually traced to Al Qaeda. The government scoffed at the notion that Al Qaeda attracted followers inside Yemen. 
But that attitude began to shift after Sept. 11. Yemen suddenly found itself mentioned in the same breath as Afghanistan as a possible target of the United States, and the damage wrought by terrorism -- especially scaring away tourists and foreign investors -- started to hurt. The government also saw an opportunity to achieve some its own goals, like bringing the tribes to heel. 
Government raids on suspected Qaeda hide-outs have resulted in the deaths of several suspects and the seizure of explosives. Officials say the government also deployed about 400 special troops, newly trained by American teams periodically sent here, at 13 bases in the three desolate northeastern provinces most hospitable to fugitives. 
Yemeni officials and Western analysts believe the number of radicals in the country is small -- probably about 20 committed terrorists and another 100 or so sympathizers who might help organize attacks.
But the assault on the French tanker Limburg on Oct. 6 proved militants can still strike here. There have also been other attacks including explosions outside the headquarters of the Political Security Organization, which rounds up terror suspects, and grenades lobbed into the American Embassy. 
American aid in the antiterrorism fight -- most of it geared to military training and creating a coast guard -- amounts to about $100 million. It includes a computerized system of tracking who crosses the borders that was introduced with mixed results -- frequent electrical failures at some crossings mean the guards do not always use the system. 
Yemeni officials protest that Washington focuses too heavily on security matters, and President Saleh is under pressure to show that cooperating with the West in a fight widely seen as against Islam will pay off with economic development. 
The depth of Yemen's commitment remains a question. The suspicions that dogged the Cole investigation -- notably that some senior officials harbor sympathy for the militants -- have never entirely faded. 
Yemeni critics accuse the government of heeding American demands while not really uprooting sources of militancy that might alienate government supporters. 
Steps that the government boasts about, like taking over hundreds of independent high schools that specialized in religious and paramilitary training, are dismissed by critics as less about terrorism than about reducing the influence of Islah, the main Islamic opposition party. 
"In Yemen you cannot eradicate terrorism by arresting 10 or 1,000 or 2,000 people; that is no solution," said Abdel Bari Taher, a Yemeni columnist formerly prominent in the Socialist party. "The solution is to stop favoring tribal domination over civil rights and to introduce an alternative curriculum for education." 
Critics say that during the 1990's about 20 percent of the 6 million students in Yemen, a country of 18 million, went through the religious high schools and now work throughout the government. But the government is seen as reluctant to undertake a purge of militant sympathizers because it owes a debt of gratitude to the most radical elements, some Afghan veterans, who fought hard against the opposition in the 1994 civil war. 
Yemen is the ancestral homeland of Osama bin Laden, and some here see him as a kind of archetype holy warrior. Hundreds if not thousands of Yemenis passed through camps in Afghanistan; Yemenis have been involved in many major terrorist incidents in recent years; and at least 53 are among the prisoners at the Guantanamo Bay Naval Station in Cuba. 
The government has begun trying to uproot Mr. bin Laden's following. His posters and tapes of his speeches were banned. More than 100 men being held in Yemeni jails because of suspected links to terrorism are undergoing a kind of religious re-education, with government clerics explaining how Al Qaeda's violence contradicts Islam. 
President Saleh summoned various tribal leaders to give them a "with us or against us" speech, winning a public commitment from most to back the fight. But soldiers trying to storm a suspected Qaeda hide-out last December were killed by tribesmen near Sheik bin Jallal's house and the two suspects remain at large. First 10 tribal sheiks were taken hostage for a month, then each had to surrender four sons or relatives. Sheik bin Jallal's 15-year-old and other relatives are being held.
Over a Yemeni supper that included bread mash infused with thick local honey, Sheik Sinan abu Luhum, one of the most revered tribal leaders in the country, said the tribes largely helped maintain stability. He quoted a Koranic injunction to dismiss the idea that tribesmen would knowingly shelter wanted terrorists. 
"Those who make war against Allah and his apostle and spread disorder in the land shall be put to death or crucified or have their hands and feet cut off on alternative sides or be banished from the land," he intoned. "They shall be held in shame in this world and sternly punished in the next." 
Yemenis feel partly maligned in the terrorism fight, and any assault on Iraq would further sour the image of the United States, leaving members of the governing party worried that they will be painted as what one called "American agents" in parliamentary elections next spring. 
But despite fervent opposition to the American policies supporting Israel and condemning Iraq, officials maintain that the terrorism struggle will endure. 
As a first step toward changing public attitudes, the government started enforcing a ban on weapons in Sana. 
Ali M. al-Fatimy, the deputy governor of Marib, searches for the right metaphor until he decides that Kalashnikovs are to Yemeni tribesmen what baseball caps are to Americans. 
"If I go someplace I take the gun like part of my clothes," he said. "If I wasn't carrying one people would look at me oddly." Guns, he asserted, do not abet terrorism and violence. 
"Look," he said, "you've been in this province what, an hour? Have you heard any gunfire?"

HEADLINE: Bear Rages Past Wall St. To Tax Coffers 

BYLINE:  By LESLIE EATON 

 BODY:
That the air has gone out of the stock market balloon is no secret to any American with a 401(k) retirement plan. But what that means for New York, economically and fiscally, is only now becoming clear. 
 And the picture is ugly. 
 Take, for example, state taxes. In April, May and June, New York State's personal income tax collections fell by 26.8 percent, compared with those from the same stretch of 2001, according to an analysis released last month by the Nelson A. Rockefeller Institute of Government in Albany. Personal income taxes account for almost two-thirds of state revenues.
Only a tiny fraction of the decline came because fewer people were working; withholding in that period edged down just 1.4 percent. The big drop -- 31.1 percent -- came in estimated taxes, which are paid by the wealthy and reflect investment profits. 
"The bubble has burst, and now we're seeing the hangover," said Nicholas Jenny, senior policy analyst at the institute.
During the long bull market on Wall Street, both the city and state governments became accustomed to collecting (and spending) hundreds of millions of dollars a year in taxes on stock profits -- which have now dried up. So have the taxes on stock options, which during the boom times companies used to pay employees. Another huge source of income was taxes on Wall Street bonuses, which dropped sharply last year and will probably fall by another $6 billion this year, according to industry estimates.
The scope of the problem was hinted at by Mayor Michael R. Bloomberg, who last week broke the code of political omerta by revealing that the city's budget for the current fiscal year has a $1 billion hole in it. Budget watchers were shocked, but mostly by the timing: most politicians seem to have been sworn to silence until after the election.
Tax revenues have already plunged, and will continue to tumble this year and probably next year as well. More layoffs loom on Wall Street, which has cut more than 20,000 jobs in the last year; Citigroup and J. P. Morgan recently developed plans to lay off 3,000 employees.
Not surprisingly, perhaps, the Federal Reserve Bank of New York reported last week that the market for expensive apartments and houses has started to weaken in New York, as has consumer spending. The loss of thousands of lucrative jobs is likely to lead to unemployment for other workers, too; each job on Wall Street supports at least two others elsewhere in the city, according to a widely accepted estimate by the Securities Industry Association, Wall Street's lobbying group. 
Meanwhile, demands on the state and city governments are likely to increase. The welfare rolls in New York City are starting to edge up, according to the Human Resources Administration, and applications for public assistance have jumped. Unemployment insurance benefits have run out for well over 100,000 New Yorkers, and it is not clear that many of them have found work.
The federal money that was supposed to help New York City recover from the World Trade Center disaster has been trickling in -- and some lawmakers are saying that millions of dollars will never arrive at all. 
All of this helps explain why budget analysts are talking about problems similar to those the city faced in the early 1990's, even though the underlying economy today is far less damaged than it was then. A decade ago, the city was reeling from the loss of almost 330,000 jobs; so far, between the recession and the terrorist attack, the city has lost about 128,000, according to the New York State Department of Labor. 
Not that there is any guarantee that the job losses have ended, though the labor picture has been stable for several months. Historically, New York has lagged behind the nation in recovering from recessions, and the national economic picture is cloudy.
"The single biggest downside risk is a double-dip U.S. recession," said Ronnie Lowenstein, director of the New York City Independent Budget Office. While economists put the chance of the economy going right back into recession without a full-fledged recovery at only one in three, she said, "that sounds pretty scary to me." 
But even without another recession, governments will still struggle, warned Robert A. Kurtter, a senior vice president at Moody's Investors Service, which rates city and state bonds. "There is a clear disconnect between the economy and government revenues," he said. "There is a governmental revenue recession going on."
The reason is that governments across the country have gotten used to raking in huge sums from their wealthiest citizens, who were making money in the stock market. In New York State, he noted, people with incomes of more than $200,000 make up less than 3 percent of tax filers -- but they pay almost half of the income tax.
In New York City, taxpayers who made $100,000 or more (there were about 200,000 of them in 1998) paid more than 60 percent of the income tax collected, according to the mayor's budget message this year.
That's because the wealthy were particularly flush with capital gains. Between 1994 and 1999, New York City taxpayers' income from capital gains quadrupled, rising to $21.8 billion from about $5.2 billion, according to the Independent Budget Office. Capital gains, once a relatively minor source of income, became the second largest after wages, which rose 40 percent during that time. 
But the stock market peaked in 2000, and by last year it had begun to plunge. For a time, the effects on tax collections of this were masked by a quirk of the system, because wealthy taxpayers file quarterly estimated taxes based on their previous year's results. But in April of this year, the ugly truth started to become apparent: not only were tax revenues low, but an unusually large number of filers were also due refunds.
State and city budget officials tried to adjust for lower stock-market profits, as well as for a weakening economy. The city, for example, forecast that capital gains would decline by 5 percent in 2002. 
But no one expected that the stock market would keep sliding, or that Wall Street would finally start slashing payrolls. Nor did it seem likely that bonuses would keep falling, after a 28 percent drop last year.
But that is all but certain to happen. Frank Fernandez, chief economist for the Securities Industry Association, said that "variable compensation," as the Wall Street firms call it, looks likely to come in 23 percent below last year's low level, and could be down as much as 30 percent. 
Capital-gains tax collections, which fell 38 percent last year, may drop further, perhaps by as much as 35 percent, he said. 
New York City is in many ways luckier than the state, because the city also collects real estate taxes, which are far less volatile than income taxes and make up a larger share of overall city tax revenues. Last year, while personal income tax revenues fell by almost a billion dollars, to $4.7 billion, real estate taxes rose by about half a billion dollars, to $8.7 billion, according to the New York City comptroller's office.
So far this year, income tax receipts are coming in just about where budget officials expected them to, but officials are worried about the future. As a rough rule of thumb, every 10 percent change in the Standard & Poor's 500 index causes a $250 million change in tax revenues, according to one budget official. Even after the recent rally on Wall Street, the market is still down about 20 percent this year.
And even if the stock market rally were to continue, "we've missed the bonus season," said Larian Angelo, finance director for the City Council. Budget experts widely expect the city to lower its forecast of tax revenues.
Data from the New York State comptroller's office indicates that personal income tax payments through September are about 24 percent lower than last year, with the big drops coming once again in estimated payments, not withholding. State budget officials who work for the governor have questioned the comptroller's calculations, noting that he is running for governor against their boss.
In any event, they say, what really matters is how revenues are holding up against the state budget forecast, and that so far taxes are coming in above expectations. But Carole E. Stone, the state budget director, did not rule out future problems. "There are potential risks to revenues" early next year, she said in a statement, "connected to bonuses, capital gains and withholding, and we continue to monitor these risks closely."
The source, the cause of those risks is pretty obvious. As the securities industry's Mr. Fernandez put it, "Virtually all the problems New York State has are from Wall Street."

HEADLINE: Homes Perched on Seawalls Hang in Balance of Dispute 

BYLINE:  By The New York Times 

DATELINE: SOLANA BEACH, Calif. 

 BODY:
Staring up at his home here, Paul Santina sees dreams collapsing. 
 He and thousands of others who own beachfront castles atop sandstone bluffs in California are watching their foundations disappear, one grain of sand at a time. From a beach that is submerged at high tide, Mr. Santina points 80 feet up to his neighbor's patio, which has hung precariously since this summer, when the bluff below it collapsed. 
 "Over the last 18 to 24 months," Mr. Santina said, "it has accelerated from one or two bluff failures a year to one or two a month."
Mr. Santina, who leads a group called the Beach and Bluff Conservancy, sees salvation in sand replenishment, artificial reefs and especially seawalls, hills of rock or concrete that reinforce the bluffs.
Environmental groups say such measures favor property owners at the expense of the public good and advocate the opposite approach, a program of "planned retreat."
California has the least restrictive seawall regulations in the country, environmental groups say. For example, Texas, North Carolina and South Carolina prohibit seawalls. This year state legislation was introduced that would make the permit process for seawalls more stringent. Faced with fierce opposition from homeowners and builders' groups, it has since been shelved, but a new bill is being prepared for next year.
The seeds for the current beach erosion were sown by bulldozers from the 1960's to the 1980's, when prime beachfront real estate was rapidly and densely developed. 
Dave Skelly, a coastal engineer, said beaches had been robbed of sand by California's 1,400 dams and reservoirs, 200 debris basins, sprawling housing tracts and miles of concrete stream diversions. Encroaching development has "narrowed the beaches to the point that there is no sand," he said.
A recent study found that more than 100 of California's 1,100 miles of coast were armored, or shored up, with seawalls or jetties -- a 400 percent increase since 1970. Environmental groups argue that such measures improperly occupy public beaches, making them unusable.
"There is no vested right to build a seawall on public property," Jim Jaffee, director of California Beach Advocates, said. 
The Surfrider Foundation, an environmental group that claims 35,000 members, says that by "fixing" the shoreline and preventing natural sand release from bluffs, seawalls contribute to erosion, creating the need for more seawalls. 
Many seawalls exist because of an apparent contradiction in the state Coastal Act. Part of the act states that property may be developed only if a seawall is not required, but another part says that if an emergency can be proved, a seawall "shall" be allowed to protect a structure.
To address this loophole, Assemblywoman Patricia Wiggins, who represents Napa County in Northern California, sponsored a bill that changes "shall" to "may." This proposed change drew opposition from homeowners and builders, who say the shift would allow the commission to let homes fall into the sea. Surfrider and other environmental groups say planned retreat, and not armoring, is the best approach. In the short term, planned retreat includes temporary seawalls and sand replenishment to protect property. In the long term, the state government would buy out beachfront property at a discount while allowing homeowners to stay for 50 years. Then the houses would be demolished and the bluffs restored to a natural state. 
Giving up an entire row of prime beachfront homes is simply untenable to Mayor Marcia Smerican of Solana Beach and many of her beachfront constituents. "You can't do it," Ms. Smerican said. "You go to the first row, the second and then what about infrastructure? Highway 101? Beach access? Restaurants?"
Surfrider says Ms. Smerican is ignoring an environmental impact report her office authorized. The report showed that erosion rates in Solana Beach equaled 27 to 40 feet every 100 years -- not enough to pass the first row of homes.
Solana Beach residents hope to save their homes by creating a "hazard abatement district," financed largely by homeowners and a bond issue. The district would allow a long seawall designed to resemble the coastal bluffs, long-term sand replenishment and underwater "sand retention" devices or artificial reefs.
The coastal commission, California Beach Advocates and Surfrider Foundation are skeptical. "Sand retaining structures keep sand on one beach by robbing it from another," Christopher J. Evans, executive director of Surfrider, said.
Last year, as part of a $17.5 million project for San Diego County beaches, financed by the federal and state governments, Solana Beach had 200,000 cubic yards of sand pumped in. The project was considered a success in many communities, but little of this sand remains here.
"Property owners want private property rights, but we, the taxpayers, have to pay these subsidies so they can live on the front row," Mr. Evans said.
Mr. Santina countered that not building more seawalls would be disastrous for the community.
"Let houses fall into the ocean, let the community's economy be destroyed, ruin tourism and let kids get killed from falling bluffs," Mr. Santina said. "We want to rebuild the beaches."

GRAPHIC: Photo: Paul Santina, left, whose Solana Beach home, above, is built atop eroding bluffs, talks with Dave Skelly, a coastal engineer. (Jack Smith for The New York Times)

 INTERNATIONAL 3-21

At Least 67 Civilians Dead After Moscow Siege Ends
Russian troops violently crushed a 57-hour hostage siege in Moscow, freeing almost 750 people from two days' captivity and ending a terrorist threat aimed at destroying President Vladimir V. Putin's Chechnya war strategy and even his political career. The civilian death toll was estimated to be at least 67. The government said 50 guerrillas were killed. 1

Russian officials said that they launched the raid because the guerrillas had begun to kill hostages, but according to official statements and witnesses' accounts, the predawn assault began more than three hours after the first two hostages were shot. 20

The mass confusion in the hours after the siege, when sick and wounded hostages were rushed to at least three hospitals in Moscow at dawn, left hundreds of relatives searching frantically for family members across the city. 20

Call for North Korea to Disarm
At the Asia Pacific Economic Conference the United States, Japan and South Korea issued a joint statement calling for North Korea to dismantle its clandestine nuclear weapons program "in a prompt and verifiable manner" and warned Kim Jong Il, North Korea's leader, that his "relations with the international community" hinged on his quick compliance. 1

France Weighs a Challenge
In a bold diplomatic challenge to the United States, France announced that it may formally introduce its own resolution on disarming Iraq at the United Nations Security Council. 8

Wellstone's Death Renews Scramble in Senate Races
The political disarray caused by the death of Senator Paul Wellstone of Minnesota put a new focus on the battle for control of the Senate, leaving strategists in both parties scrambling in a national struggle that remains too close to call little more than a week before Election Day. 1

As Democrats huddled in St. Paul to begin the delicate process of replacing United States Senator Paul Wellstone on the Nov. 5 ballot, associates of Walter F. Mondale, the former vice president, said he was likely to heed the growing pleas from party leaders to join the campaign. 32

The Search for the Snipers
The police had feared a bloody end to the 23-day manhunt in the Washington-area sniper shootings. But the peaceful arrests of John Allen Muhammad and John Lee Malvo brought an abrupt coda to an investigation full of dead-end leads followed by a rapid reversal of fortune. 1

The man who co-owned the blue 1990 Chevrolet Caprice that has been linked to the sniper attacks was taken into custody in Michigan. 35

An Emory University professor resigned after an academic panel released a report strongly critical of his research for a widely debated book about the history of guns in America. 26

As members of the United States Olympic Committee prepare to choose between New York and San Francisco as their candidate for the site of the 2012 Summer Games, they say they are considering factors that range from how the cities treat their favorite sports to their feelings about a sympathy vote for New York. 39

Tax Losses Equal Budget Woes
Budget analysts are talking about problems for New York City similar to those faced in the early 1990's, even though the underlying economy today is far less damaged than it was then. HEADLINE: In Search of Good Health? Try a Bad Attitude. 

BYLINE:  By HENRY FOUNTAIN

 BODY:
NOT to sound too negative about it, but there's yet more evidence that this power-of-positive-thinking idea may not be all it's cracked up to be.
 Survey findings released last week at a meeting of the European Society of Medical Oncology show that cancer support groups do not help patients live longer. Edzard Ernst, a professor of medicine at the University of Exeter in Britain, and colleagues reviewed earlier studies on the issue and found no evidence for the belief, held by some doctors and patients, that the good vibrations gleaned from talking with others who are in the same boat can do as much to prolong life as any physical treatment. 
 The researchers were quick to point out that they have no doubt that positive thinking helps patients feel better and improves the quality of their lives. It just doesn't add days to them. 
That may be a bitter pill for those who urge the grouchy and grumpy among us to cheer up. Norman Vincent Peale, who convinced millions that worry, doubt and defeatism can be overcome, might be overcome himself were he alive to hear the news.
All in all, this hasn't been a bull market for positive thinkers. Two decades ago, in the Reagan era, it might have been morning in America, but now, with terrorism, snipers, threats of war and economic anemia, it's the middle of the night. We're tossing and turning, anxious and angry thoughts racing through our heads, while somewhere a faucet goes drip, drip, drip.
Do we really need to look on the bright side?
Barbara S. Held, a psychologist and professor at Bowdoin College, argues that it's time to end what she calls the "tyranny of the positive attitude in America."
"It's gotten worse since 9/11," she said. "There's less tolerance for the expression of negativity. You're labeled unpatriotic, as if you've somehow given in to the enemy."
Negativity is not such a bad thing for some people, Dr. Held said, from the rude curmudgeon who wallows in it to the normally upbeat person having the occasional bad day. "Insisting that they smile and look on the bright side may backfire and make them feel even worse," she said.
(Want proof that negativity works? Look no further than Jimmy Carter, the man who preceded Mr. Reagan and whose 1979 "malaise" speech is perhaps the most public display of negativity in recent memory. He was just awarded the Nobel Peace Prize.)
"There's much to be gained from appreciating our negative thoughts and feelings," said Gerald Amada, a psychologist and the former director of the mental health program at the City College of San Francisco. It is important to acknowledge that negative thoughts can serve a purpose. "We need to take a morally neutral position with respect to angry emotions, not always see them as destructive and all-consuming," said Dr. Amada, the author of "The Power of Negative Thinking" (Madison Books, 1999).
Even when it seems that negative thoughts should dissipate -- as with the apparent resolution last week of the sniper case in the Washington area -- there are those who will stay gloomy, angry or anxious.
"Some people might say, 'What are you angry about -- he's been caught,' " said Dr. Held, whose book "Stop Smiling, Start Kvetching" was published last year by St Martin's Press. "But now there's a whole new dimension to life. There may be more snipers. Life is more unstable than we ever conceived of."
But perpetually anxious people have their own form of bulletproofing. Their Kevlar consists of setting expectations low, preparing for the worst possible outcome and precisely planning how to prevent it from happening.
Julie K. Norem, a psychologist and professor at Wellesley College, calls this defensive pessimism. "This focuses them on things they can do, and away from the anxiety," she said. "An awful lot of people do learn how to do it for themselves in a concrete way."
ANGER can help some people manage anxiety, too. "One of the things that makes anxiety so awful is that you feel out of control," said Dr. Norem, the author of "The Positive Power of Negative Thinking" (Basic Books, 2001). "Anger is an energizing emotion. With anger you feel more in control."
Whether venting anger or maintaining a generally glum attitude has actual health benefits, however, is an open question, Dr. Norem said, although she pointed to one study that showed a correlation between cheerfulness in men and earlier mortality.
"You don't want to make too much of it," she said. "But for cranks like me, it was encouraging."

HEADLINE: Candidate Learns Limits of 9/11 Resume 

BYLINE:  By JOHN RATHER 

 BODY:
THIRTEEN months ago Joe Finley of Fort Salonga was just another firefighter commuting between a home in the Long Island suburbs and a firehouse in the city.
 These days he is a Republican candidate for Congress, testing whether residents of Long Island's Second Congressional District will show their esteem at the ballot box for a fireman who rushed to the World Trade Center on Sept. 11 and stayed on to toil in the ruins. 
 Mr. Finley, 46, a third-generation city fireman who has never run for public office before, became the first to try to parlay his experience of 9/11 into a political career. But the campaign has languished, and it appears overwhelmingly likely that he will be rejected by the voters. 
Mr. Finley has done all the things candidates do in an otherwise conventional campaign hampered by limited money. He strongly backs President Bush's policies on Iraq, believes decisive action against global terrorists is long overdue, views a vibrant economy as the best defense against terrorism, and supports heightened domestic security. He has laced into Steve Israel, the Democratic incumbent, calling him a spin doctor who "tries to play both sides of the fence" and "wants to be all things to all people."
Sensitive to perceptions that he might be exploiting the mantle of heroism worn by all city firefighters, Mr. Finley has rejected the hero label. His intent, he said, is to continue in Congress the service he could no longer perform as a firefighter because of lung damage sustained during the trade center rescue effort. 
"The heroes from 9/11 are the 343 firefighters and the police officers and first responders who were lost that day," he said. "I am just somebody that went there and tried to help." 
But beginning on the day in June when he announced his candidacy from the firehouse at 29th Street and Second Avenue in Manhattan where he has worked for the past 12 years, he has repeatedly invoked his firefighter family and 9/11 credentials.
Mr. Finley, who took an unpaid leave from the department to run, said he was convinced he would win if voters knew of his service. His problem, he and others said, was getting out the word while running a very low-budget campaign.
His low profile with voters stood out in a survey Global Strategy Group of Manhattan did for Mr. Israel earlier this month. 
The survey showed Mr. Israel leading Mr. Finley by more than two to one and found that Mr. Finley was "still completely unknown" to the great majority of district voters. Results were based on the responses of 400 likely voters in the Second District, which includes all of the Town of Huntington and parts of Smithtown, Islip, Babylon, Oyster Bay and Hempstead towns.
Mr. Israel, 44, of Huntington, who is running for a second term, said he had raised more than $1 million in the past two years and would spend it on "a 100 percent positive campaign." Mr. Israel said Mr. Finley was due "respect and honor." But he said voters wanted a congressman "who has the experience and the leadership to make a difference on issues that affect their everyday life."
"This election is about each candidate's positions and achievements on specific issues," he said. He said his record showed his work to improve health, economic and Social Security, and to secure $40 million in federal money for constituents.
Mr. Finley has received some national media attention because of the 9/11 connection. But Amy Walter, a political analyst for the nonpartisan Cook Political Report in Washington, said Mr. Israel appeared almost a presumptive winner.
"Had Joe Finley been a TV repairman this would never have been a story that got national attention," Ms. Walter said. "But I see nothing to suggest that Steve Israel is vulnerable."
"To run simply as somebody who was involved in the rescue effort of Sept. 11 is a nice profile, but it's not enough substance to be elected to Congress," she added. 
Mr. Finley and Mr. Israel do have differences. Mr. Finley favors allowing workers to put a portion of Social Security taxes into personal retirement accounts. Mr. Israel opposes what he calls privatizing Social Security. Mr. Finley said Mr. Israel has been soft in his support for President Bush's antiterrorism and Iraq policies. Mr. Israel describes himself as "one of the most hawkish Democrats in the House of Representatives."
Political analysts and politicians said the shape of the reapportioned Second District was yet another disadvantage for the Republican challenger. Solidly Republican areas along the South Shore are now in the Third Congressional District, strengthening the hold of the incumbent Republican Peter T. King, while the Second District gained Democratic voters. The district was so uninviting that the Republicans' initial choice, Allan Binder of the Suffolk County Legislature, noted it as a reason for withdrawing from the race.
Richard H. Schaffer, the Suffolk Democratic Party chairman, said Mr. Finley was running a one-dimensional campaign.
"We all, including Steve, would agree that we thank him for his service," he said. "But certainly he hasn't mentioned anything beyond that."
Mr. Schaffer said it was "probably cynical political handlers" who told Mr. Finley to emphasize being a firefighter at the World Trade Center.
But Mr. King said running as a hero was nothing new in American history. "You have guys who were war heroes, John Glenn who was an astronaut, and decorated cops," Mr. King said. "Joe is a very solid guy. I certainly wouldn't say he's exploiting anything."
Anthony Apollaro, the Suffolk Republican chairman, said Mr. Finley "probably listened to a lot of the same kind of advice that John Kennedy did."
The only other New York firefighter running for Congress is opposing Mr. Finley and Mr. Israel. John Keenan, 41, a lieutenant from West Sayville, is the Green Party candidate in the race.
Unlike Mr. Finley, Mr. Keenan has hardly mentioned his firefighter status or the time he spent at the trade center. "When I first announced my campaign everybody asked me what was the 9/11 connection," he said. "My reply was that it has nothing to do with my decision to run."
"I lost brothers too," he said. "Just because I don't talk about it doesn't mean I care less."

HEADLINE: The Nation: Lost Hurrah; 
A Senator's Death and the Fight for Congress

BYLINE:  By ADAM NAGOURNEY

DATELINE: WASHINGTON 

 BODY:
SENATOR PAUL WELLSTONE, the Minnesota Democrat who died in a plane crash on Friday, was a partisan Democrat and proud of it. In the Senate, he was the guardian of the left, a man "of conviction, who never swayed from his beliefs, even when he was fighting a lonely battle," said Senator Phil Gramm, the conservative Republican of Texas.
 It was not much of a surprise this month when Mr. Wellstone -- in the midst of a tight re-election battle that he very well might have lost -- defied the Democratic leadership and joined 22 other senators in voting against President Bush's Iraq resolution. 
 Mr. Wellstone was the most unusual of politicians running for re-election these days: happy to reside on the edge of the political scale, to take potentially damaging stands. As such, his death highlighted what seems to have been missing from both parties in the desultory exercise that has passed for a campaign for Congress this year.
The midterm election of 2002 should be one of the most captivating nonpresidential campaigns in 20 years. Republicans believe they can take back the Senate, perhaps more so after the tragedy of Mr. Wellstone's death, and Democrats believe they can win back the House. There are tight contests for governor taking place in a half-dozen of the most populous states.
The economy is unsteady, and the nation is talking about a war in Iraq. Also, this is the first national election since the Sept. 11 attacks, which leaders in both parties thought would revive public attention to elections and government.
Yet as Election Day creeps slowly, and almost unnoticed, upon the country, this election has stirred little interest. 
"The intensity is not high," said Marc Racicot, the Republican Party chairman, with understatement.
The midterm campaign has been competing, in a losing bid for attention, with a barrage of compelling events, from the debate over Iraq to the hunting down of the sniper outside of Washington to the crash of Mr. Wellstone's plane. There is little evidence that Americans are paying much mind to who is seeking to lead them.
But why should they?
Since at least 1992, when Bill Clinton won the White House by, in part, appropriating traditionally Republican issues, the nation's two political parties have increasingly sounded the same notes during campaigns.
If the Republicans were left at the gate in 1992, they have surely caught up this year, blurring the lines on everything from prescription drug coverage to corporate malfeasance to the handling of Social Security.
Democrats and Republicans are lamenting the prospect of another election with low voter turnout, but in truth, they have only themselves to blame. What initially had been seen as a clever, if perhaps cynical, gambit for political advantage has ended up giving voters a choice between beige and brown.
Mr. Wellstone was the exception. He knew when he voted against the Iraq resolution that it would become an issue in his campaign. By contrast, consider what has transpired in this campaign, at least outside the boundaries of Minnesota. 
The Democrats have sought to turn the election on domestic issues and the economy; the Republicans have sought to make it a debate, to a large extent, about foreign policy and national security. But for the most part, the response of the opposition party has not been to engage in a real debate, but to go along -- or at least appear to be going along -- with exercises in legislative sleight-of-hand.
A voter examining the scene this year would have a hard time "telling the difference between these two parties and why they should support one over the other," said Rachel Gorlin, a Democratic media consultant. 
"There is a major definitional question about what constitutes a Democrat, and what a Democratic platform is, and what are the differences between the candidates in this new world of ours," she said.
This fall, Republican candidates are talking tough about corporate wrongdoers and advancing plans that would subsidize prescription drug costs for seniors -- and they are doing so with the vigor of, well, Democrats.
In Maryland last week, the Republican candidate for governor, Rep. Bob Ehrlich, who has a long record of voting against gun control legislation, attacked his Democratic rival, Lt. Gov. Kathleen Kennedy Townsend, for the state's failure to check the criminal backgrounds of prospective gun purchasers.
FOR more than a year, many Republicans, including Mr. Bush, had advanced the idea of funneling part of Social Security proceeds into private investment accounts. The last place to look this year for a debate on whether this money should be invested in the private market -- an intriguing enough idea -- is on the campaign trail.
On the other side of the aisle, the Democratic Party leadership quickly fell in line behind Mr. Bush when he requested authorization to oust Saddam Hussein, despite severe misgivings by many members.
For all the talk about the potency of the economy as an issue, Democrats have, with a few notable exceptions, steered far away from what is probably the sharpest point of contrast between the two parties: Mr. Bush's $1.3 trillion tax cut, and what a few Democrats argue is the damage it has done to the nation's economy. (And yes, Mr. Wellstone voted against, and spoke out forcefully against, the tax cuts.)
"In the end, the Democrats haven't been able to say, 'We have a different point of view,' " said Andrew Kohut, director of the Pew Research Center, a polling institute.
Democrats argue that their biggest problem this cycle has been trying to break through the roar of competing stories.
"The way the media works today, it's kind of a pack story of the day and things start up sometimes slowly and than you get a fever pitch on something like the sniper story, and it kind of pushes aside everything else," said Rep. Richard A. Gephardt of Missouri, the House minority leader. "Now that that's over, I think the economy dominates the stage."
Yet even some Democrats suggested that the reason the party wasn't getting heard was that it didn't have much to say. 
"I don't think they had a coherent plan for how to do it -- except to say it or to wish it," Ms. Gorlin said of an economic plan by fellow Democrats. "But the platform to take us there -- well, I never saw it."
Inevitably, the homogenization of American politics favors the status quo, even during disquieting times, which is why an increasing number of analysts believe it is unlikely that the Democrats will win back the House -- and now may even lose their one-seat hold on the Senate.
Some Democrats are surprised by how this race seems to be turning out. But in the end, why should they be? Why throw out the rascals for a new cast of rascals? 
At least with Mr. Wellstone, Minnesota voters knew what they were getting.

HEADLINE: Leadership Struggle Inside Canada's Governing Party Intensifies 

BYLINE:  By CLIFFORD KRAUSS 

DATELINE: TORONTO, Oct. 26 

 BODY:
The parliamentary election is more than a year away, but the campaign to replace Prime Minister Jean Chretien entered its first critical phase this week with ministers from the governing Liberal Party sharpening their sabers for a rough internal struggle for the party leadership.
 With the economy performing well, no overriding issue has emerged to replace the defining issues of recent campaigns like sovereignty for Quebec and free trade with the United States. 
 But a robust debate on political ethics is starting. It comes after a series of cabinet resignations this year prompted by disclosures that government contracts had been given to ministers' friends and contributors to the Liberal Party. Ethics is viewed as the principal vulnerability for the Liberals, although the main center-right and leftist opposition parties appear too splintered to take full advantage at this point. 
The leading contender to replace Mr. Chretien, former Finance Minister Paul Martin, has been shielded from the disgust with the party's ethics. Mr. Martin was forced to resign in June for political reasons and he is seen as a rival to Mr. Chretien. But Mr. Chretien remains a powerful force within the party, and he publicly challenged Mr. Martin after the former minister gave his first major policy speech of the campaign, calling for sweeping political changes in the parliamentary system.
Mr. Martin has a big campaign war chest and an effective grass-roots organization throughout Canada, but a handful of challengers have emerged in recent days who appear ready to attack him as too conservative to lead the left-leaning party.
Heritage Minister Sheila Copps, a dark-horse candidate, blamed Mr. Martin's 1995 budget cuts for soaring college tuition. "The pendulum is swinging back," she said in an interview in The Globe and Mail. "One of the values of a national government is educational accessibility."
Mr. Martin's chief accomplishment as finance minister for eight years was balancing the federal budget in 1995 after years of deficits. The policy helped lower interest rates and inflation but also cut spending for education, housing and other social services. Her call for a "debate of ideas" was viewed as a call to revive the party's traditional policy of generous spending.
Since Mr. Chretien announced in August that he would step down in 2004 rather than run for a fourth term, he has tried to keep the jockeying to succeed him in check. On Tuesday, he dropped his insistence that cabinet members refrain from raising money or organizing for a party leadership convention scheduled for November 2003.
Organizers for Ms. Copps, Deputy Prime Minister John Manley and Industry Minister Allan Rock began working to challenge Mr. Martin.
In a speech on Monday, Mr. Martin signaled that he intended to break from Mr. Chretien's tight-fisted control of the House of Commons, promising to give backbenchers more independence and power over legislation. He said it was unacceptable that the overriding question in Ottawa relevant to getting things done was "Who do you know in the P.M.O.?" -- the prime minister's office.
Mr. Chretien shot back: "I think that Mr. Martin knew somebody in the P.M.O. that was pretty important. It was myself. And he did pretty well with me."
For the opposition, the top candidate to emerge is Stephen Harper of the center-right Canadian Alliance party. He is considered a long shot unless he can make a coalition with the Progressive Conservative Party.

HEADLINE: THE 2002 CAMPAIGN: THE HOUSE; 
In 4 Races, an Incumbent Is Guaranteed to Lose

BYLINE:  By ADAM CLYMER with PAUL ZIELBAUER 

DATELINE: COLLINSVILLE, Ill., Oct. 25 

 BODY:
Ordinarily, being a member of the House of Representatives is one of the safest jobs in America. With the advantages of free mailings, free travel and political action committee money, 96.7 percent of incumbents were re-elected in 1996, 1998 and 2000 combined.
 But this year, at least four incumbents will lose the general election. In four districts, incumbents are running against other incumbents because of lost House seats and redistricting, the political equivalent of downsizing. Here in southern Illinois, where cornfields become St. Louis suburbs, the loser will be Representative David Phelps, a Democrat who says, "I'm for working America; he's for corporate America," or Representative John Shimkus, a Republican who says, "I never apologize for voting to cut taxes." 
 There are such pairings in Connecticut, Mississippi and Pennsylvania, and only in the central Pennsylvania district around Harrisburg does the Democrat, Representative Tim Holden, appear to be ahead.
While no single small set of districts will determine control of the House, campaign officials in both parties agree that the uphill road Democrats face in these 4 prominent races emphasizes the difficulty of their challenge. If Republicans do win 3 of these 4, that means the Democrats will need a net gain of 9 in the other 35 or so competitive races to control the House.
Howard Wolfson, executive director of the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee, said Democrats had one substantial advantage in the Connecticut, Illinois and Pennsylvania races -- well-organized get-out-the-vote efforts. But while Mr. Holden has managed to stay financially competitive, all three Democrats are being outspent.
In Connecticut's new Fifth District, the legislative mapmaking made for an even start. It has equal parts of the old districts of Representatives Nancy L. Johnson, Republican of New Britain, and Jim Maloney, Democrat of Danbury. 
In Mississippi, action by a federal court and inaction by the Justice Department on a plan by the Democratic Legislature produced a district that the Republican, Charles W. Pickering Jr., cannot lose to the Democrat, Ronnie Shows. The Pennsylvania district was created with more of Representative George W. Gekas's old district than of the Democrat Tim Holden's old district.
Here in Southern Illinois, not only is 60 percent of the new 17th District old Shimkus territory, but that territory falls in the St. Louis media market, where Mr. Phelps needs to introduce himself but cannot afford to advertise. Only the Democratic Congressional Campaign Committee is broadcasting his praises here, while Mr. Shimkus, the National Republican Congressional Committee and the drug industry through the United Seniors Association are promoting the Republican on television.
Mr. Shimkus and Mr. Phelps are firm supporters of gun ownership and opponents of abortion. Mr. Shimkus is smoother; Mr. Phelps, a singer and songwriter, is folksier.
Meeting with the farm bureau in Edwardsville on Wednesday, Mr. Shimkus argued that he was no rubber stamp for the House G.O.P. leadership, saying he did not want Congress to recess before adopting an energy bill, drought relief and a domestic security measure. In Godfrey on Thursday, Mr. Phelps insisted the issue was the economy, and denounced Republicans for opposing an increase in the minimum wage. 
Mr. Shimkus started slowly, but he claims his most recent poll gives him a 52 to 36 percent lead. The Phelps camp argues that it is much closer, as does Andrew Theising, assistant professor of political science at Southern Illinois University at Edwardsville. But Mr. Theising says Mr. Phelps will need an exceptional Democratic turnout to have any real chance. 
Representative Tom Davis of Virginia, leader of the N.R.C.C., calls Pennsylvania's new 17th District the most Republican of the three seriously contested member-to-member races, and says it is the only one his side might lose.
The reason is the candidates. Mr. Holden, 45, has outhustled Mr. Gekas, 72. He told retired federal employees on Monday, "This election is about the future of the people the new 17th Congressional District."
Mr. Gekas helps that argument along when, in response to charges that his past votes sought to end cost-of-living increases for Social Security, he once said that happened in the time of Julius Caesar. Another time he said it was in Charlemagne's era. He has been in Congress for 20 years.
Mr. Holden calls himself a conservative on social issues and an advocate of a balanced budget. But he said the 2001 tax cut favored the rich and called the Republican prescription drug bill a sham. 
Mr. Gekas played the piano for senior citizens at the Shoemakers V.F.W. hall on Tuesday and assured them that Social Security was safe from any cuts or delays in payments. 
At a Republican dinner in Millerstown, he praised the president for pushing foreign trade. That mattered, he said, "not just because it creates jobs, not just because it bolsters the enterprise instinct of Americans, but because it will sustain the economy, build the economy, grow the economy and thus be able to feed and empower our armed services for the struggles that lie ahead."
The Holden camp says its polls show him leading by 10 percentage points. G. Terry Madonna, a pollster and professor at Millersville University, finds that plausible and says of Mr. Gekas: "Every now and then legislators run the string out. They get so removed that people don't think they care enough."
Mr. Holden and Mr. Phelps are helped by Democrats at the top of their tickets running far ahead in their races for governor, Edward Rendell in Pennsylvania and Rod Blagoyevich in Illinois. 
Mr. Maloney gets no such help in Connecticut, where Gov. James G. Rowland, a Republican, seems a safe bet for re-election.
Various public polls show Mrs. Johnson, moderate Republican, running 16 to 18 percentage points ahead. Her 10 terms on the House Ways and Means Committee have given her a marked fund-raising advantage. As of Oct. 16, Mrs. Johnson had raised $2.9 million, compared with $1.67 million for Mr. Maloney; she also had about $1 million on hand, compared with slightly more than $450,000 for Mr. Maloney.
In a race between two tough incumbents whose records are more alike than opposite, in a district with more independent voters than either Republicans or Democrats, most experts agree that her money has made the crucial difference. 
While Mr. Maloney has sought to portray Mrs. Johnson as more concerned with the well-being of large health maintenance organizations than their patients, she has criticized him as a back-bencher with only a small fraction of her influence in a Republican-led House. 
"It's very important for your congressman to be a very strong voice," Mrs. Johnson, the leader of the Ways and Means subcommittee that oversees Medicare and Medicaid spending, told an audience at a debate with Mr. Maloney in Southbury, Conn. 
But Mr. Maloney points to her 20-year tenure as evidence that she is beholden to special interests. He renewed his promise to retire after his next term, his third, if he wins it.

HEADLINE: U.S. and 2 of Its Allies Warn North Korea on Atomic Arms 

BYLINE:  By DAVID E. SANGER and TIM WEINER 

DATELINE: CABO SAN LUCAS, Mexico, Oct. 26 

 BODY:
The United States, Japan and South Korea issued a joint statement here today calling on North Korea to dismantle its clandestine nuclear weapons program "in a prompt and verifiable manner" and warned Kim Jong Il, North Korea's leader, that his "relations with the international community" hinged on his quick compliance with the demand.
 The statement was issued after considerable behind-the-scenes diplomacy by the United States to form a common front with the Asian allies against North Korea's project to enrich uranium in its latest attempt to develop atomic weapons. But it stopped short of threatening the complete economic isolation of North Korea that the Bush administration has talked about in the last week. 
 The statement said Prime Minister Junichiro Koizumi of Japan, who traveled to North Korea to normalize relations just before the nuclear disclosure, warned today that "normalization talks would not be concluded" until the nuclear issue was fully resolved. That statement was considered crucial by American officials because Japan is the largest potential source of trade and investment for North Korea. 
The statement came as the administration seemed increasingly anxious that members of the United Nations Security Council -- including Mexico, the host of the Asia Pacific Economic Cooperation summit meeting that started here today -- are still unwilling to give Washington a broad latitude to strike at President Saddam Hussein of Iraq. 
The French said they would introduce their own resolution if the Security Council does not reach a consensus on the Bush proposal. Page 8. 
President Vicente Fox of Mexico told President Bush today that his country was still consulting with France and Russia, among other nations. Mexico holds a pivotal vote on the Security Council as it debates how to confront Iraq. 
Mexico, France and Russia have pursued an alternative resolution that Mr. Bush finds too constraining, and he warned anew today with some frustration in his voice that he has told the world "in speech after speech after speech" that "if the U.N. won't act, if Saddam Hussein won't disarm, we will lead a coalition to disarm him."
Underscoring the president's impatience with the six-week-long debate in the United Nations, Secretary of State Colin L. Powell said today that "we have reach the point where we have to make a few fundamental decisions in the early part of next week" about whether the United Nations is ready to pass a resolution that Mr. Bush believes will make it clear to Mr. Hussein that failure to disarm will result in an attack. 
"If resolution is not possible," said Secretary Powell, who had argued strongly this summer that any war with Iraq would have far more legitimacy if the United States first went to the United Nations, "then let's come to that realization and move forward."
Mr. Bush's effort today to manage the twin crises of Iraq and North Korea was a study in two very different diplomatic approaches. The president and his spokesman, Ari Fleischer, made it clear that the White House had just about run out of patience with the continuing debate over Iraq, and even the more patient Secretary Powell said "we can't continue to have a debate that never ends."
But on North Korea, where administration officials have concluded that no real military option is available because of the North's huge array of conventional weapons within easy reach of Seoul, the South Korean capital, Mr. Bush seems content to engage in the kind of slow consensus building that his critics say has been missing from the Iraq debate. In the 10 days since the White House made public North Korea's disclosure that it has violated a 1994 agreement to freeze its weapons in return for Western energy help, American diplomats have toured Asia, Russia and Western Europe to piece together a common declaration that a North Korean nuclear weapon could not be tolerated.
Administration officials make no secret of where they are headed: They want to win agreement on an economic isolation of North Korea, already one of the world's least developed and most isolated nations, if it refuses to dismantle the new nuclear project. An older nuclear program, involving the production of plutonium at a nuclear reactor site called Yongbyon, appears to have remained inactive.
The United States is not talking directly to the North Koreans after the meeting early this month where the existence of the program was disclosed, Secretary Powell said today. But Japan and South Korea are going ahead with previously scheduled talks. A senior Japanese official here said today that "we have no plans to give economic assistance to North Korea" before the nuclear issue and the question of abducted Japanese citizens are resolved.
Secretary Powell was equally forceful on that point after the North Koreans, in a statement on Friday, said they would negotiate about the program only if the United States signs a nonaggression pact. "You can't violate an agreement and then show up and say, 'We violated this agreement, what will you pay us for this violation in order to get out of the violation,' " he told reporters. 
When that issue came up in the 1990's, North Korea said part of the deal is the withdrawal of all American troops from South Korea, which today number around 37,000. In the statement today, however, Mr. Bush repeated an assurance he made in February shortly after calling North Korea part of the "axis of evil" -- that the "United States has no intention of invading North Korea."
Part of the American strategy seems to be to wait for South Korea's president, Kim Dae Jung, to leave office in a few months. Mr. Kim, a Nobel Prize winner, built his legacy on engagement with the North, and he seems more reluctant to follow the path of isolation. Today, he said very little in public.
But not all of those here are convinced of the urgency of the problem. The Russian prime minister, Mikail M. Kasyanov, said here on Friday that North Korea and its newly disclosed nuclear weapon program posed no danger to the world.
"We do not have any evidence and proof that North Korea holds any threat," he said.
Mr. Bush arrived in this sealed-off resort early today, under bright blue skies after a hurricane blew through 180 miles away. He met his host, President Fox, for a conversation that was dominated by the American counterterrorism agenda and issues of immigration that seemed on the verge of making progress before the Sept. 11 attack. But after the meeting, their seventh in 22 months, the two men appeared to be at an amiable impasse.
Mr. Bush said he recognized Mr. Fox's desires for legal rights for undocumented Mexicans in the United States. But the two did not reach any accord on that issue, or on the question of heavily subsidized American agricultural imports, which threaten to swamp Mexican farmers and businesses, driving even more migrants north.
Nor did Mr. Bush win Mexico's pivotal support in the Security Council vote on Iraq. Mr. Fox remained noncommittal on the American desire for a resolution that could lead to military action against Iraq.
The leaders will announce on Sunday an accord aimed at achieving "'the twin goals of enhanced security against terrorist threats and continued facilitation of the movement of goods, capital and people." According to a copy of the agreement, they will "freeze the assets of terrorists and their supporters without delay," block them from "accessing the international financial system," and "investigate and prosecute money launderers and terrorist financiers." 
In the focus on terrorism, many of the past initiatives of the meetings seemed almost ignored. There was little discussion of global financial stability efforts, which dominated these meetings during the financial crises of the 1990's, particularly the Asian collapse in 1997 and 1998. There was surprisingly little talk of Japan's deepening banking crisis, and little noticeable pressure on Mr. Koizumi to fix the system after 10 years of decay. "We've got a lot of fatigue on that subject," one American official said. Even trade, the reason the group came into existence, seemed a passing thought.
Instead, terror dominated. Mr. Kasyanov, standing in for President Vladimir V. Putin, who stayed home to deal with the aftermath of the hostage crisis in Moscow, said world events compelled "the countries which were to some extent reluctant to join in this coalition to more actively participate in combating all signs of terrorism."
Mr. Bush also met with Indonesia's president, Megawati Sukarnoputri, aiming to stiffen her spine against militant Islamic forces in Indonesia. It took the bombing in Bali this month, which killed some 200 people, for her to acknowledge the force and depth of that militancy in predominantly Muslim Indonesia.
