HEADLINE: THREATS AND RESPONSES: ATTACK ON U.S. DIPLOMAT; 
AMERICAN ENVOY KILLED IN JORDAN

BYLINE:  By NEIL MacFARQUHAR 

DATELINE: AMMAN, Jordan, Oct. 28 

 BODY:
A senior American diplomat in Jordan, Laurence Foley, was gunned down outside his house today, shot eight times at close range in what appeared to be the latest of a string of terror attacks against Western targets in the region and beyond. 
 The assassination reverberated through this often sleepy capital, not least among the expatriate community. Foreign residents said they had been rudely awakened to the realization that the potential for escalating violence on both sides of Jordan -- in the West Bank as well as in Iraq -- could roil a city long considered safe. 
 Since the Sept. 11, 2001, attacks in the United States, American embassies around the world have frequently closed their doors in response to threats or warnings of possible attacks. But to a large extent, American missions abroad rely on the vigilance of their employees, repeatedly cautioning them to be alert to their surroundings. 
"Since that day, Sept. 11, we have been under severe security alerts and concerns repeatedly," said the United States ambassador here, Edward W. Gnehm.
The United States Embassy in Amman was closed after the attack today and a warning notice was issued to employees and to Americans living in Jordan. "The one today reminded people that it was very important that they look after their personal security in a very vigilant way," Mr. Gnehm said.
He refused to comment on who might be behind the assassination, saying it was too early to speculate about any group or motive. 
Both Jordanian and American officials said it was too soon to determine whether there might be any link to Al Qaeda or other militant Islamic groups.
In Washington, the State Department spokesman, Richard A. Boucher, said, "At this point, we have to know a little more before we start to describe it as terrorist." He also said that it seemed to fit the pattern of other deadly attacks at so-called "soft" targets this year, including a church in Pakistan and a nightclub in Indonesia.
"All over the world, we've seen this terrible practice that when we are better able to protect what you might call the hard targets," he said, "they tend to go looking for other things." 
Analysts were quick to point out that numerous small militant groups unearthed by security forces in Jordan in recent years, including operatives linked to Al Qaeda, harbored virulently anti-American and anti-Western sentiments. 
About half of Jordan's residents are Palestinians, and many Iraqis live there as well. The widespread perception in the Arab world that the United States has been biased on the side of Israel, and the opposition in the region to the prospect of an American military attack on Iraq have compounded the tension. 
Rebecca Salti, an American who has lived in Amman for three decades, spoke of a widespread sense in the region that Washington remained in a heedless confrontation with Arabs and Muslims, and said this was creating "a political earthquake."
"There are victims who have to pay the price," she said. "Who is going to be next?"
Jordanian investigators said that Mr. Foley was shot with a 7-millimeter pistol around 7:15 a.m., and collapsed in his driveway next to his maroon Mercedes with its diplomatic license plates. His wife, Virginia, discovered his body in a pool of blood and alerted the police.
The American Embassy said that Mr. Foley, who was from Oakland, Calif., had been working in Jordan since August 2000 as a senior administrator of America's development assistance program here. He had recently celebrated his 60th birthday and just on Sunday received an award for his aid work.
Ambassador Gnehm, his voice breaking as he noted Mr. Foley's long years of service to the United States, said his government was outraged by what he described as a "heinous act." 
Neighbors getting their children ready for school this morning said they heard neither gunshots nor the usual barking that the Foleys' golden retriever directed at passing strangers. "It's something weird, we didn't hear anything," said Osama Rafidi, a jewelry store owner who lives across the street. "We didn't hear gunshots, we didn't hear cars, we didn't hear anyone running, nothing."
Mr. Foley's neighbors noted that there were occasionally embassy guards outside the house, a two-story white limestone building with magenta bougainvillea spilling over the walls. But they said no guards were in evidence this morning.
There are about 9,000 American citizens in Jordan -- most of them dual nationals -- registered with the embassy, said a spokesman, Justin H. Siberell. Although an Israeli businessman was killed in Mr. Foley's neighborhood last year, the killing today was the first known killing of a Western diplomat in the Jordanian capital.
"The war on terror seems to be breeding more, not less," said Rami Khouri, a Jordanian columnist. "There is no sign that anyone in the U.S. or Israel or the Arab world is addressing the grievances that lie underneath all this."
While protesters against a war in Iraq have marched in the West, the repressive regimes of the Arab world have blocked most demonstrations. 
Experts on political Islam also said it was impossible to tell from any of the recent incidents whether they were ordered by some revived Qaeda network or simply individuals acting on their anger. 
"The idea that you should hit the interests of the West is widespread enough for us to take into consideration that there are individuals who don't need a network," said Francois Burgat, a French expert on Islamist groups, when asked about previous attacks this month in Kuwait, Yemen and Indonesia.
The ambassador and several other American diplomats live behind the fortress-like walls of the new embassy compound. Those who live outside, like the Foleys, are protected largely by 24-hour random security patrols that pass by their homes, Mr. Siberell said.
American diplomats are assigned license plates numbered 108 or 109, but Ambassador Gnehm noted that those who did not want them could get ordinary plates. Both Mr. Foley's Mercedeses parked in his driveway and a red Jeep Cherokee parked in front of the house had plates numbered 109. 
Mr. Gnehm said there had been no threats immediately prior to the attack, although the embassy had warned its staff and Americans in Jordan repeatedly since Sept. 11 last year to be extra vigilant. He said he was confident the Jordanian security services could solve the crime, and was satisfied with the security provided by the government, which he said would be enhanced.
Muhammad Adwan, the Jordanian information minister, said in a statement: "The attack, regardless of its motives, is an attack on the country and its national security." 
Mr. Foley was the chief administrative officer for the Agency for International Development in Jordan and a veteran of similar programs in Bolivia, Peru and Zimbabwe. He first worked for the government in 1965 as a Peace Corps volunteer in India. 
Mr. Foley and his wife have three grown children and two grandsons. 
Mrs. Foley had a private practice offering psychological counseling to Jordanians and expatriates, and was also helping the embassy to list the ways the United States was helping Jordan, her friends said. 
In his remarks, Ambassador Gnehm quoted Mr. Foley as telling his wife the night before he was killed, "I am where I want to be, doing what I want to do."
Marcia Rhodes, a water aerobics instructor and a friend of Mrs. Foley, described Mr. Foley as "like a big teddy bear that you just wanted to hug." 
Mrs. Rhodes and her husband, Curt, who works for a group that helps Jordanian street children, said they had lived through the Israeli siege of Beirut in 1982 and the frequent car bombings there, finally leaving when Americans began being kidnapped. 
"If there was equity and fairness on the other side of the Jordan River, then this kind of thing would not happen," Mr. Rhodes said. "I can't tell you that I am not shocked and scared. Some guy may decide tomorrow that morning that I'm the one he wants to target."

DIPLOMATS AT RISK
Attacks on American Envoys Abroad

The most recent overseas killings of full-time American Foreign Service employees:

GEORGE TSANTES -- A military liaison at the United States Embassy in Athens; shot and killed, apparently by a terrorist group, in Athens in 1983.

A. A. SCHAUFELBERGER III -- A military attache at the United States Embassy in San Salvador; killed at a sidewalk cafe in that city in 1983.

ENRIQUE CAMARENA SALAZAR -- A United States Drug Enforcement Administration agent occupying a State Department post in Mexico; kidnapped, tortured and killed by a Mexican drug cartel in Guadalajara in 1985.

WILLIAM F. BUCKLEY -- C.I.A. station chief in Beirut, Lebanon; held captive and later killed in Beirut by Hezbollah in 1985.

WILLIAM E. NORDEEN -- A Navy captain and the military attache in Athens; killed by a car bomb there in 1988.

ARNOLD L. RAPHEL -- Ambassador to Pakistan; killed in an air crash, possibly caused by a bomb, in Pakistan in 1988. (The crash also killed Pakistan's president, Mohammad Zia ul-Haq.)

HERBERT M. WASSOM -- A brigadier general and the military attache in Pakistan; killed in the same plane crash as Mr. Raphel.

GARY C. DURELL -- A communications technician; killed by gunfire near the American Consulate in Karachi, Pakistan, in 1995.

JACQUELINE K. VAN LANDINGHAM -- A secretary; killed in same incident as Mr. Durell.

HEADLINE: RETRACING A TRAIL: THE SUSPECTS' TRAIL; 
$2 Bud, and a Bundy for Every Bar Stool

BYLINE:  By CHARLIE LeDUFF 

DATELINE: BELLINGHAM, Wash., Oct. 28 

 BODY:
Serial killers drank here, they say at this bar on Holly Street.
 Its name is the Waterfront Tavern. It is a good bar that should not be judged bad simply because some customers misbehaved so spectacularly, patrons said today. 
 That is what they said in the mid-70's after Ted Bundy drank here. Mr. Bundy confessed to killing 20 women.
They said it in the late 70's, after Kenneth Bianchi drank here before he was arrested in the killing of two college roommates. He and a cousin, Angelo Buono Jr., were later convicted in the Hillside Strangler case in Los Angeles.
They said it over a year ago, after James A. Kinney, who also drank here, was arrested in the murder of a woman found dead off the Mount Baker Highway. He pleaded guilty. 
Now they are saying John Muhammad, accused in the sniper case, drank here.
"I don't know, we just seem to attract them," said Millie Ulmer, the early morning bartender. "This town is full of drifters, but even so, Muhammad didn't look right to me. Little did I know."
Bellingham is a jumping-off place to the Alaskan tundra and the Canadian wilds. The area is home to hippies, yuppies, militiamen, fishermen, American Indians and tramps. People brew applejack in the woods and breed bird dogs.
The tavern opens at 6 a.m., the earliest in town. It has bay windows, cheap paneling and a view of Bellingham Bay. A pint of Budweiser goes for $2 in the morning. All this is what attracted the other men, say regulars who say they remember Bianchi and Kinney and recount local Bundy lore. 
The bar is a stone's throw from the mission where Mr. Muhammad and his teenage traveling companion, Lee Malvo, stayed. Drinks are cheap. Mr. Muhammad drank Budweiser as he whiled away mornings watching cable news and "The Price Is Right."
"He looked clean, and he was very polite and articulate," said Lynne Farmer, the owner. "He always had his own money. He applied for a job, but we threw the application away like we do with all guys from the mission."
Mr. Muhammad did not try to blend in or adjust his personality to charm the locals, as Ted Bundy did. He was belligerent, fellow patrons say; he railed against the United States and spoke up for the World Trade Center attackers.
"I wanted to take him outside when he was cheering for 9/11," said Drew Samdilands, who was drinking this morning before he took his boat out to lay crab pots. "I had a gut feeling about the guy and I wanted to kick his head in, but they probably would have put me in jail."
Despite the guest list, people who drink at the Waterfront described themselves as warm, well-adjusted. Perhaps it was their accepting attitude, they surmised, that let so many notorious characters feel welcome.
"Live and let live," said Pete Davis, who is unemployed. "Of course, we only know after the fact that they're bad guys. I don't know. Maybe we should name sandwiches after them."

CORRECTION-DATE: November 1, 2002

 CORRECTION:
An article on Tuesday about the Waterfront Tavern in Bellingham, Wash., where patrons say serial killers have visited, referred incorrectly in some copies to the spot where the body of a victim of James A. Kinney was found, and referred incorrectly to the view from the bar. The scene was off the Mount Baker Highway, not Interstate 5. The bar overlooks Bellingham Bay, not the ocean.

HEADLINE: THE MEDIA BUSINESS: ADVERTISING -- ADDENDA; 
Aegis Reports Gains From Broadcast Ads

BYLINE:  By Bernard Stamler 

 BODY:
The Aegis Group in London said it was reaping the benefits of "further clear improvements" in broadcast advertising in the United States since the first half of the year and that it expected results for the year before one-time costs to be "significantly better" than in 2001.
 The statement from Aegis contrasts with last week's announcement by the WPP Group in London that its revenue was falling largely because of the poor economy in North America. 
 Aegis also said that it would appeal a ruling against a subsidiary, Carat USA, in New York State Supreme Court on Friday. A jury awarded $6.8 million to Independent Media Services in New York, which contended that Carat violated a noncompetition agreement when it participated in a review for the account of New Line Cinemas in New York, a division of AOL Time Warner.

HEADLINE: TWO CITIES; 
Where the Jobs Are

BYLINE:  By SETH FEASTER

 BODY:
 Only two fields dominate the top of the Labor Department's list of fastest-growing occupations over the rest of this decade: health care and computers -- including the software that drives them and the networks that link them. 
But a look at the areas that will add the greatest numbers of jobs gives a different view. Here, lower-paying service jobs, like those at fast-food restaurants or in retail sales, top the list alongside the better paying jobs in computers and health care. The job market also varies significantly from state to state, and more surprisingly, perhaps, can vary widely even between nearby cities in the same state. SETH FEASTER

HEADLINE: Well-Received 'Harlem Song' May Close by Week's End 

BYLINE:  By ROBIN POGREBIN 

 BODY:
Despite strong reviews, "Harlem Song," the variety show at the Apollo Theater that chronicles Harlem's history through song, dance, photographs and film, announced yesterday that it would close at the end of the week unless $300,000 could be raised.
 The show, performed Saturdays through Mondays, opened in August and was scheduled to play through the end of the year, with the hope of becoming an annual engagement. But the $4 million production has been running at a loss since it opened -- most recently about $30,000 a week short of the $200,000 it needs to break even -- and the producers said they could not afford to continue. 
 The show has been considered a cornerstone of Harlem's revitalization, which has brought Harlem Starbucks, Old Navy and former President Bill Clinton; it has been seen as particularly important to the Apollo's current $53 million restoration. 
"It generated a whole new constituency and audience base for the Apollo," said David D. Rodriguez, executive director of the theater. "It created tremendous momentum." The show was created by George C. Wolfe, producer of the Joseph Papp Public Theater and director of landmark productions like "Angels in America" and the Pulitzer Prize-winning "Topdog/Underdog."
Producers often post a closing notice in the hope that some investor might save the day. John Schreiber, lead producer of "Harlem Song," said he and his partners were no exception. 
"We believe this show is too important to the city of New York to close," he said, and added: "A corporation, foundation, individual has an opportunity now to be a hero or a heroine and enable this really terrific experiment to continue."
In trying to explain the show's financial struggle, its creators cite a lack of money for marketing and the psychological barrier to drawing downtown New Yorkers up to Harlem for a night on the town. "People are still getting over the fact of going up to Harlem," Mr. Wolfe said. "Hopefully, Manhattan can keep expanding its definition of what comprises Manhattan."
"Harlem Song" has run through its $500,000 reserve as well as $750,000 from the Time Warner Foundation and the New York City Investment Fund, a private fund started by Henry R. Kravis. When the producers approached the city and state for help, they were directed back to the Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone, which turned them down, Mr. Schreiber said. 
In many ways the show has been a success, drawing more than $2 million in ticket revenue and an estimated $4 million in ancillary retail and restaurant income to the area, according to the producers. One dollar of every ticket sold has been donated to the Harlem Strategic Cultural Collaborative, a consortium of nine arts organizations, including the Boys Choir of Harlem and the National Black Theater.
The show has sold more than 50,000 tickets over the last four months to an audience that is 10 percent to 15 percent tourists; mostly from outside the neighborhood; and 65 percent black and 35 percent white. "Our desire at the get-go, to get rid of that invisible boundary between Harlem and the rest of the city, is succeeding on many levels," Mr. Schreiber said. 
"We have shown people from all over New York City and domestic and foreign tourists that uptown is a great place to visit," he continued, "a great place to see a show, a great place to have dinner and a great community to get to know."
Because "Harlem Song" has been running at a deficit, the producers have not had money to market the show, they said; they cannot afford to broadcast a television spot that features Whoopi Goldberg and would cost $100,000 to $200,000 to broadcast.
Mr. Schreiber said that if the show closes it would be more difficult to bring it back next year, in part because those who have purchased advanced tickets will be left disappointed. The show has an advance of about $900,000 through the end of the year; half of that is from group sales.
The show will also have to abandon its education program; a study guide, just recently completed, was developed with the Schomburg Center for Research in Black Culture.
Even if the show closes, Mr. Schreiber said it may have touring productions and be broadcast on television. Sony is due to release the cast album before Thanksgiving. 
Arts executives and business owners in the area said they would be sorry to see "Harlem Song" end. Michael Eberstadt, the owner of Bayou, a Creole restaurant, and Slice of Harlem, a pizza shop, in Harlem, said his sales had been up 200 percent to 350 percent on weekends and Mondays because of the show.
"One of the really good things that's happened is 'Harlem Song,' " Mr. Eberstadt said. "It should be doing much better than it is."
Lowery Stokes Sims, director of the Studio Museum in Harlem, said the show had benefited other cultural organizations like hers. "It was starting to bring tourism down from the bus and onto the ground," she said. "This is the kind of neighborhood tourism that we were interested in fostering."
The producers failed to gain the financial support of the Upper Manhattan Empowerment Zone, one of nine distressed areas designated by the Clinton administration for public financing and tax incentives to spur private investment.
Because "Harlem Song" is a for-profit venture, the producers were asked to apply for aid through the nonprofit Apollo, a producer partner in the show. Nevertheless the application for $1.2 million in empowerment financing was rejected. "They did not respond to us positively," said Charles Flateman, the show's executive producer. 
Terry Lane, president and chief executive of the zone, did not respond to two messages seeking comment. 
Although she is a fan of the show, C. Virginia Fields, the Manhattan borough president, said "Harlem Song" did not necessarily qualify for empowerment money. "I'm not sure that is the type of project that falls under the empowerment zone purview," she said. "Most of what the empowerment zone has been funding is job creation on an ongoing basis, as opposed to any particular program or service."
Leonard Garment, president of the Jazz Museum of Harlem, who has applauded "Harlem Song," said the show was precisely the type of venture the zone was meant to support. "It's the kind of thing the empowerment zone should be doing," he said. "That's what it was chartered to do."
Representative Charles B. Rangel of Harlem said he would like to be of help. "Whatever I can do I want to do," he said, adding, "I was hoping the empowerment zone could see its way clear to investing in this."
Gordon J. Davis, founding chairman of Jazz at Lincoln Center, said he was very disappointed to hear that "Harlem Song" may close. "Every day I go around New York, people ask me about it, and I say, 'It's terrific, you should go,' and they say, 'Yeah, I've been meaning to go, I've been meaning to go,' " he said. "And once they know about the closing notice, all those people will say, 'Oh, I wish I'd gone, I wish I'd gone.' "

HEADLINE: TRADE WINDS; 
Global Trade in Elmwood Park: Familiar Saga With a Twist

BYLINE:  By ANTHONY DePALMA 

DATELINE: ELMWOOD PARK, N.J. 

 BODY:
WHEN legislation making it easier for the United States to enter new free-trade agreements was passed last summer, reactions were black and white.
 President Bush called the trade promotion authority bill, formerly known as "fast track," "a victory for the American economy" and dreamed aloud of free trade pacts with Chile, Singapore, Australia and Morocco, the nations of southern Africa and the democracies of the Western Hemisphere. The President sees trade as a means of increasing exports and creating more than enough jobs to compensate for those that will inevitably be lost to foreign imports. 
 Unions doubt it. John J. Sweeney, the president of the A.F.L.-C.I.O., calls fast track "a blank check to send good jobs overseas." 
But the picture from this industrial city, and in particular from the site of a former factory that produced televisions for the JVC Corporation, is anything but black and white. Six years ago, the factory closed and its operations and 198 jobs were moved to Tijuana, Mexico, partly to take advantage of incentives that were created with the 1994 signing of the North American Free Trade Agreement, but also because of the $3.50 daily wages Mexican workers generally receive. 
Here, new jobs eventually emerged after JVC's departure, though not as many jobs that were lost. And the reason for those new jobs had less to do with the expansion of global free trade than with the expansion of another local business, a successful one with a convenient but overcrowded location.
At first, the loss of JVC was a heavy blow for this compact borough of 18,000 people on the banks of the Passaic River in Bergen County, about 11 miles west of New York City. Suddenly gone were not only the jobs but also $180,000 in taxes that the property rendered annually. "Sure, we were worried," said Richard A. Mola, who has been mayor for 31 years, "but what could we do?"
The move caught JVC's workers by surprise. "The last Christmas before JVC closed, the president came and said there was no chance that the plant was going to move," said Janina Zylik, who had spent eight years on the assembly line, fitting electronic works into television cabinets.
"Then a week before the layoffs they told us that was the end," added Mrs. Zylik, a native of Poland who is 62 years old. "All they said was that they were moving to Mexico."
Many JVC workers collected unemployment after the plant closed. Some went for retraining through the government assistance program. Mrs. Zylik's daughter, Beata, who also worked at JVC, took maternity leave, and has remained at home to raise her family.
Nelson Silva operated a forklift at the plant and was shop steward for Teamster Local 810, which represented most of the JVC workers in Elmwood Park. He is now vice president of Local 810 and considers himself "one of the lucky ones," because he found a new career.
"But for many others it was horrible," he said. Guillermo DeLeon's options were limited. When he lost his job on JVC's shipping line, his wife was six months pregnant. He said he would have liked to have gone to school to learn computers or pick up some other skill, but he needed to find a new job with health benefits immediately. He collected unemployment for two months before landing a job at the Sharp Electronics Corporation in Mahwah, N.J., for slightly more than the $9 an hour he had earned at JVC. 
"Just covering the cost of having the baby was difficult," said Mr. DeLeon, 41, who lives in Paterson. He later bounced from job to job looking for better pay or greater security. Finally, in 1998, Local 810 helped him and two other former JVC employees find warehouse jobs with, of all companies, JVC, which has a distribution center in Pine Brook, N.J.
Only a few of the JVC workers in Elmwood Park actually lived in the borough, which for much of the last century was named East Paterson because of its proximity to that big-shouldered city and the industries that thrived there. In more recent years, however, the community became uncomfortable with the industry it once embraced.
By the mid 1960's, being linked by name to Paterson and its rusting mills caused embarrassment. In 1973, the borough of East Paterson voted to change its name, choosing Elmwood Park because it seemed serenely suburban and it enabled the police to keep the same initials -- E and P -- on their cars. 
In keeping with this notion, some industrial land in Elmwood Park was subsequently rezoned as commercial space, suitable for the hotels and office buildings that matched the community's suburban aspirations. 
But it was industry that continued to pay much of the borough's freight. Right on Market Street, for instance -- and near the entrance to the JVC plant -- is one of the borough's largest taxpayers and its biggest employer, Marcal Paper Mills.
Marcal has been making tissues, paper towels and toilet paper in Elmwood Park since 1939, growing so strongly that in the mid-90's the company planned a major expansion. 
There was one big problem. Marcal was hemmed in by the Passiac River, Interstate 80 and residential streets. The company was bursting out of its headquarters, an old silk mill from an era that had also faced competition from cheap imports. 
The solution turned out to be next door. When JVC's three television assembly lines were shipped out, most of the 175,000-square-foot building was emptied. The company continued to use a small section as office space, but in 1998 moved its corporate headquarters to Wayne nearby, leaving the building vacant. 
Marcal agreed to buy the JVC plant and its 11 acres. By shifting warehousing operations there, Marcal freed up enough space in the main manufacturing building to install a $70 million papermaking machine. The improvement meant Marcal could expand a line of bathroom tissue products that brought in new business for the company, which recorded $300 million in sales last year.
"JVC was a dream come true for us," said Nick Marcalus, who with his brother Peter operates the company that was started in 1932 by their grandfather, Nicholas Marcalus. Without obtaining JVC's space, he said, Marcal would have had to expand elsewhere in the country.
"That acquisition gave us a chance to expand our business here," Mr. Marcalus said. "Without that building, it would have been imprudent for us to put in a new paper machine and expand our output." 
Marcal did not hire many former JVC workers, Mr. Marcalus said, because more than two years elapsed between the end of JVC's manufacturing operation and the start of Marcal's expanded production line. 
It did hire Janina Zylik. 
Though stunned to be suddenly out of work when her former employer moved south, Mrs. Zylik did not even apply for the trade adjustment assistance for which she was eligible. Her husband, Mieczyslaw, already was a machine helper at Marcal. She took off six weeks to have a knee operation, then applied for a job at Marcal. She was hired to work on the packaging line, and is now paid about $12 an hour, $4.50 more than at JVC. 
"It's a good company," she said of Marcal. "Whoever wants to work can work." 
Mrs. Zylik's production line handles bathroom tissue produced on the machine that Marcal installed after acquiring JVC's building. The machine is largely computerized, with a few supervisors overseeing the work. 
Marcal has put on 100 additional workers to package, ship and sell the increased work, Mr. Marcal said. That number is equivalent to about half of the employees displaced when JVC left. With the additions, Marcal's total employment is 1,000 workers, most unionized, on 88 acres. 
Marcal's move into the old JVC space also ensured that local delicatessens and convenience stores hardly noticed JVC's absence.
In assessing the effect of moves like JVC's, Daniel T. Griswold, associate director of the Center for Trade Policy Studies at the Cato Institute, a libertarian research group, said that trade had little long-term impact on the overall number of jobs because the American economy tended to create jobs in more sophisticated industries to replace those that are lost. Moreover, he added, televisions are manufactured more efficiently and cheaply in Mexico.
"That means that an American family that goes into Wal-Mart is going to get a television for a lower price or one of a higher quality at the same price," Mr. Griswold said. "When 198 jobs in New Jersey are lost, that makes headlines, but the fact that millions of American families can more easily afford a television set doesn't."
Guillermo DeLeon, who now works in the JVC shipping department, does not see it that way. "There we are, packing and shipping televisions, VCR's, DVD players from all over the world that could have been made here," Mr. DeLeon said. He thinks that the additional free trade deals that President Bush wants to sign will only increase the exodus of manufacturing jobs in the United States. "This is not good," he said. 
Nick Marcalus said he supported expanding free trade, but disagreed with what he considered hasty decisions of other manufacturers to leave the United States for cheaper wages across the border. 
"Unfortunately," Mr. Marcalus said, "many companies that have moved out of the Northeast underestimated the ability of the work force to learn and adapt."

Aid for Displaced Workers: Is It Enough?
THE first federal law to help workers hurt by trade was enacted in 1962 and modified several times since. In 1994, the North American Free Trade Agreement provided a new set of benefits that applied only to workers who could prove they were displaced because their employers moved to Canada or Mexico. 
According to a report by the General Accounting Office in 2000, the number of workers covered by both Nafta and the general trade assistance program averaged 167,000 annually between 1995 and 1999. The report also found that while most workers who received assistance, which averaged $870 a month for up to one year, found new jobs, about half took at least a 20 percent pay cut. 
In a trade promotion authority bill that passed last summer, the Nafta benefits were eliminated because the two programs were combined into one. Coverage was extended to farmers and some secondary workers, and the coverage period was extended from a year to 78 weeks. 
Displaced workers age 50 or older were declared eligible to receive additional payments if their new jobs paid less than their previous ones. And workers who lose jobs because of free trade will be eligible to receive tax credits to cover 65 percent of the cost of health insurance coverage. 
But in the heated debate leading to the bill's passage, other proposals were dropped. These included extending benefits to secondary workers hurt by a plant closing -- for instance, those who might have made plastic parts for a television cabinet -- and to service-industry employees and contract workers who typically held jobs in high-technology companies hit hard by the economic downturn.
Senator Max Baucus, Democrat of Montana, who sponsored the assistance extension legislation and helped get the trade promotion authority bill (formerly called the fast track bill) passed in the Senate, has said that tens of thousands of additional workers will be eligible for benefits under the new law. But Fred S. Azcarate, the executive director of Jobs With Justice, a national group that advocates workers' rights, said that if the President can use the trade promotion bill to get new trade deals through Congress, the new adjustment benefits would fall far short.
"Workers should be extremely worried," Mr. Azcarate said. ANTHONY DePALMA

HEADLINE: The Greatest Threat 

BYLINE:  By NICHOLAS D. KRISTOF; E-mail: nicholas@nytimes.com

 BODY:
The scariest place in the world right now is not Iraq, but rather the Korean peninsula.
 We're being blackmailed by a nuclear power, and so President Bush is in an exceptionally difficult situation -- one that he has handled very ably so far. But the administration's game plan to isolate North Korea is, as our allies are desperately trying to tell us, potentially catastrophic. 
 President Bush wants to squeeze North Korea into abandoning the uranium enrichment program to which it recently confessed. We're almost certain to cut off fuel oil shipments to North Korea. 
Unfortunately, the moment we cut off oil shipments and apply economic pressure, North Korea may kick out international inspectors at its nuclear plant in Yongbyon. Then it may threaten to "uncan" the plutonium rods there, preparatory to using them to make nuclear bombs.
North Korea has enough plutonium in Yongbyon to rapidly make at least five nuclear weapons, possibly more. That's its leverage: threatening to turn Yongbyon into a nuclear assembly line, which in turn might ultimately lead Japan and South Korea to go nuclear as well.
So play the scenario out. We cut off fuel oil and introduce sanctions. Then North Korea revives Yongbyon and threatens to uncan the plutonium. From there, it's easy to imagine the U.S. bombing Yongbyon (both the first Bush and the Clinton administrations had contingency plans to do just that), after which North Korea lashes out with artillery at Seoul.
I called Kim Myong Chol, a North Korean unofficial spokesman, who said his country would respond to sanctions not just by reviving Yongbyon, but also by ramping up weapons exports.
"North Korea can export missile and nuclear technology to the highest bidder," he noted dryly. "It's a capitalist practice."
"If America tries to knock out Yongbyon," Mr. Kim added, "North Korea will retaliate immediately on New York and Washington, wipe out South Korea, wipe out Japan."
(I observed to Mr. Kim that I was speaking from Midtown Manhattan. "Well okay," he said obligingly, "maybe not New York. Washington and Chicago instead.")
In fact, North Korea's ballistic missiles probably can't reach the continental U.S. Still, North Korea's artillery can destroy Seoul. Don Oberdorfer, in his book "The Two Koreas," cites an estimate from a former American commander in South Korea that a war could kill one million people, including 100,000 Americans.
Dear Leader Kim Jong Il may also feel that the focus on Iraq gives him more room to push the U.S. around.
"North Koreans firmly believe that the U.S. cannot now make a military strike, because South Korea is a hostage and because of the Iraqi situation," said Han Park, an American scholar who has often visited the North. "That may make North Korea a bit more reckless."
So what should we do?
Donald Gregg, a former ambassador to Seoul who is president of the Korea Society, says imposing sanctions on North Korea "would be crazy." Likewise, a military strike is not feasible, given that it would probably trigger a new Korean war.
On the other hand, how can we accept a North Korea with a large nuclear arsenal? How can we continue to ship fuel oil to the North as if nothing had happened?
That leaves only one alternative, holding our nose and negotiating a deal with North Korea (without ever calling it negotiating, and possibly using proxies like China). The North would give up its nukes and missiles, all sides would agree to end the hostilities of the Korean War (there never was a peace treaty), and Western countries would normalize relations with the North. 
"The way to distinguish the hawks from the doves in this administration is the speed with which a person realizes you have to talk to the North," explains a longtime player. "The doves are already there. The hawks are struggling with, and resisting, the notion."
In the coming months, the most delicate problem in international relations will be how to negotiate an end to this crisis. If all sides play their cards wisely, we could not only defuse the confrontation, but also launch North Korea on a path like the one China pursued away from Stalinism.
North Korea is the most totalitarian country in the world, and possibly the most dangerous adversary we face. But that's precisely the reason we need to engage it.

HEADLINE: Cigna to Feel Major Loss In Customers 

BYLINE:  By MILT FREUDENHEIM 

 BODY:
The Cigna Corporation said yesterday that it would lose at least 530,000 members this year and surrender its longstanding position as the nation's third-largest health insurer.
 By January, Cigna will cover fewer than 12.7 million people, well behind UnitedHealth Group, which has 16.2 million, and Aetna, with 14.4 million. It will also trail Wellpoint Health Networks, which recently grew to 13.1 million members. 
 Cigna has lowered its official membership count to 13.25 million people from 14.15 million, based on what it said was more accurate information. And membership is dropping 4 percent to 5 percent after some acknowledged bad management decisions. 
"There were just some flat-out poor judgments," H. Edward Hanway, the chairman and chief executive of Cigna, said in a conference call with analysts to discuss the company's third bad-news announcement in five days.
Shares of Cigna fell 81 cents yesterday, to $38.58, on top of a 38 percent loss on Friday. The company said on Thursday that its earnings would be lower than expected in 2002. On Friday, it lowered its profit estimate for 2003.
James Stewart, the chief financial officer, said the company would take a relatively modest $100 million after-tax charge in the fourth quarter for costs of revamping. For example, Cigna is adding employees to improve service to customers, reversing layoffs earlier this year after new computer programs failed to work out and complaints multiplied.
Cigna said it lost $40 million to $50 million this year after it cut prices and the costs for thousands of new members were higher than expected. When Cigna then raised its rates, some of these customers switched to other insurers, Mr. Stewart said.
The company said that more than half the loss came on the worst case, which analysts identified as the Dade County school employees in Miami. A consultant to the Dade schools said Cigna lost $50 million there.
Analysts said Cigna would face perilous times through next year and beyond, which could result in the replacement of Mr. Hanway and his team, as happened after financial crises at Aetna and Oxford Health Plans. 
Tom Goetzinger, a health care analyst at Morningstar, said that Cigna had "announced five charges within a month, including more than $1 billion involving businesses they aren't in any more."
"They are beginning to look like a company that doesn't know what it's doing," he said.
Mr. Stewart said Cigna had decided to dip into "internal funds," and cancel previously announced plans to borrow $200 million, to help offset heavy charges it had incurred in its business of selling reinsurance to other companies to cover their risks in writing variable annuities.
Mr. Hanway said the decision not to borrow now was based in part on Cigna's debt-to-capital ratio, which is approaching 32 percent, above the company's goal of 25 percent to 30 percent. He said Cigna planned to pay down part of the debt next year.
Noting that several credit-rating agencies have raised questions about Cigna's finances, Mr. Goetzinger said, "they may need to borrow more money at more expensive rates." Higher interest payments could affect profitability.
Company officials noted that, unlike Aetna and Oxford, Cigna is still making money, albeit in reduced amounts, in all its lines, including health care. But they acknowledged that Cigna's problems would extend into next year.
Two-thirds of the company's health care customers have already signed contracts for 2002, many at rates that Cigna now regards as underpriced.
While some of this business is ending, and the company says it is solving its computerized information system problems, Roberta Walter, an analyst at Merrill Lynch, said she expected "both issues to persist into 2003."

HEADLINE: THE 2002 CAMPAIGN: SENATE; 
New Hampshire Rivals Look to Independents

BYLINE:  By PAM BELLUCK 

DATELINE: MANCHESTER, N.H., Oct. 24 

 BODY:
The most potent campaign commercial so far in New Hampshire's neck-and-neck Senate race is one that in other states might be considered milquetoast. 
 An advertisement for the Democratic candidate, Gov. Jeanne Shaheen, features her opponent, Representative John E. Sununu, pledging his loyalty to his party, saying he has "never done anything that I thought politically wasn't in the interest of the Republican Party." 
 In some states, that might be a plus. But in New Hampshire, there is a premium on independence. 
The number of unaffiliated voters keeps rising year by year -- it is now 37 percent of the electorate -- and polls show the two candidates virtually even, with Mr. Sununu leading in latest survey by 48 percent to 46 percent, well within the four-percentage-point margin of sampling error.
So although this race may determine which party controls the Senate, the candidates have made a point of courting independent voters by emphasizing their differences with their parties.
Mr. Sununu's spokeswoman, Julie Teer, rattled off six issues on which he disagreed with the Republican leadership, notably financing for the Superfund toxic-waste cleanup and other environmental programs. By contrast, Mr. Sununu himself said in an interview here:
"Jeanne Shaheen was asked on national television to name one issue on which she differed with party leadership. All she could come up with were regulations under the Clinton administration, and that has nothing to do with the current Democratic leadership."
Ms. Shaheen countered in a subsequent interview: "That's the difference between John Sununu and myself. I haven't spent a lot of time looking at where the Democratic leadership is on issues. But there are a couple of big issues where I disagree with Tom Daschle. When John Sununu says he's never done anything that wasn't in the best interest of the Republican Party, he wasn't kidding. That's been his record even when it wasn't in the best interests of the people of New Hampshire."
Polls suggest that Ms. Shaheen's strategy has helped her. After trailing since the September primary, she has pulled even.
A poll this month by the University of New Hampshire had her leading by three percentage points among all voters, 47 percent to 44 percent, and by 15 points among independents, 49 percent to 34 percent. The most recent poll, taken Oct. 20-22 by the American Research Group, a Manchester group, showed Mr. Sununu ahead by two points, 48 percent to 46 percent, with 6 percent undecided. 
"The gap is closing, and it started with Shaheen running the ad having Sununu saying he always supports the Republican Party," said Dick Bennett, president of American Research Group. "That was the first thing that really stuck with the independent voters."
Both candidates are respected, well-known politicians with long political legacies in the state. 
Ms. Shaheen, 55, is in her third term as governor after six years in the State Senate, and analysts attribute her electoral success to her ability to portray herself as a moderate in a state where only 26 percent of the voters are Democrats.
Mr. Sununu, 38, is a third-term congressman whose father, John H. Sununu, was governor of New Hampshire and then chief of staff to the first President Bush. 
"They are both very good politicians, they are both savvy, they both won re-election twice," said J. Mark Wrighton, a political science professor at the University of New Hampshire. "It really is a battle of heavyweights."
Earlier this year, Mr. Sununu pulled off an unusual feat, beating an incumbent senator, Robert C. Smith, in a grueling primary. While he raised less money than Senator Smith and faced some accusations of disloyalty, he won support from prominent Republicans inside and outside New Hampshire who were convinced that Mr. Smith was strident and out of touch.
It was during the primary that Mr. Sununu made his statement about his support for the Republican Party, a stance that probably helped mollify some critics. But he retains some enemies among Smith supporters.
Mr. Smith endorsed him but has been noticeably absent on the campaign trail and turned down a White House invitation to attend a Sununu rally, saying he had a family commitment.
Mr. Sununu is conservative on fiscal issues and opposes abortion except in cases of rape or incest or danger to the pregnant woman's life. 
Ms. Shaheen supports abortion rights, but also President Bush's tax cut and his policy toward Iraq. 
"The tension over Iraq that Republicans have been using in other races, she's really neutralized that," said Linda Fowler, a professor of government at Dartmouth College. "That's always been the thing that she's very adept at, which makes Republicans crazy. She takes issues that are hard for Democrats and takes them off the table."
While both candidates are considered effective campaigners, there are stylistic differences. "Shaheen has learned to create a presence when she walks in a room, but Sununu could walk in a room and you wouldn't notice him," Mr. Bennett said. 
At a recent campaign stop at a health clinic in Newmarket, Ms. Shaheen was personable, chatting enthusiastically with the nurses.
On the campaign trail in Manchester, where Mr. Sununu was meeting Latino business owners with State Representative Carlos Gonzalez, the congressman's low-key style was on full display.
At Star Fashions, Mr. Sununu quietly handed the proprietor a flier from his back pocket, said, "I'm walking through with Carlos; appreciate your vote on Election Day," and left, humming softly to himself.
What schmoozing he did was unobtrusive, almost shy.
And on the streets of downtown Manchester, Mr. Sununu did not stop to shake hands with passers-by, walking so quickly that he blew right past Tom Evans, who called a greeting to the congressman while unloading wares from his truck. Mr. Sununu doubled back and said, "I'm John Sununu." Mr. Evans said: "I know who you are. I just wanted to shake your hand." 
To which Mr. Sununu replied: "I'm not the kind of politician who goes around assuming people know who I am. It's not good form."
Ms. Shaheen, who had no primary fight, has raised more money than Mr. Sununu, but both candidates are getting hefty helpings of support from their parties and national interest groups.
In the campaign, Ms. Shaheen has focused on appealing to several influential constituencies: Republican-leaning women who support abortion rights; older voters concerned about health care; environmentalists, and small-business operators who have been hurt by the sluggish economy.
Mr. Sununu accuses Ms. Shaheen of flip-flopping on issues. He particularly criticizes her record on financing education, an issue that has dogged her since 1997, when she proposed a statewide sales tax after the courts said the schools' reliance on property taxes could penalize poor districts.
When the Legislature rejected the sales tax, the governor's solution was an interim statewide property tax, not terribly popular in this antitax state.
Interviews with voters suggest that both candidates are making headway with their arguments. 
Jayme Hansen, 44, of Somersworth, who runs a window-repair business, is a Republican-leaning independent who said he would support Mr. Sununu. "I have to go with Sununu, who has been there in Washington," he said. "I haven't been impressed with what the governor did for the state over the past six years. Education in this state is terrible." 
Another independent voter, Kylah Riendeau, 23, a student at the University of New Hampshire in Durham, said she would vote for Ms. Shaheen, partly because of the abortion issue. "I don't agree with the idea of a sales tax, but I don't think Sununu better represents us either," she said. "I feel Sununu doesn't represent a lot of my interests, and Shaheen's the lesser of two evils."

HEADLINE: Pataki, McCall and Friends Stump in New York City 

BYLINE:  By JAMES C. McKINLEY Jr.

 BODY:
The Republican and Democratic candidates for governor scrambled for votes in New York City yesterday, pulling out powerful allies to help them campaign.
 Gov. George E. Pataki toured Republican enclaves in the city and Nassau County with Rudolph W. Giuliani, the former mayor, while Comptroller H. Carl McCall got a boost from Senator Hillary Rodham Clinton at a fund-raising breakfast. 
 The Independence Party candidate, Tom Golisano, had intended to campaign in the city as well, but remained in Rochester because of mechanical problems with his private airplane, his aides said. 
Mr. McCall, trailing in polls and going into debt, also unveiled a sharp-elbowed television advertisement accusing the governor of having no principles. "When you stand for nothing, you'll stand for anything: mediocrity, failure, even corruption," Mr. McCall says in the 30-second spot. Mr. Pataki called the ad "unfortunate" and said that people wanted new ideas instead of "negative personal attacks."
The governor seemed relaxed and confident on his whirlwind tour of the metropolitan area with Mr. Giuliani, acting more like a man on a sightseeing trip than someone in the middle of a competition. He and the former mayor had breakfast with local businessmen in Woodside, Queens, and then spoke to high school students at nearby Christ the King High School about American values.
The two men, once bitter political enemies who made peace last year, shared a game of boccie and scarfed down hot dogs with a group of elderly citizens in Franklin Square, on Long Island. Then they tried their hand at making pizza at a restaurant in New Hyde Park. They took a walk along commercial strips in Brighton Beach, shaking hands, patting children on the head and speaking to crowds. They also shook hands with commuters at Grand Central Terminal, rode the Staten Island Ferry and held a political rally in Gravesend with Democrats for Pataki.
At every stop, Mr. Giuliani praised the governor for his handling of the crisis after the Sept. 11 attack and said the state needed his experience as it confronts possible future terrorist attacks, economic troubles and a budget shortfall.
"Without Governor Pataki, we would have not gotten through the last eight years and certainly not from Sept. 11 on," Mr. Giuliani said in Franklin Square. "He really is a terrific human being."
Mr. McCall, too, brought out a popular politician, Senator Clinton, to give his flagging campaign a helping hand, at a breakfast for female supporters, including the poet Maya Angelou, at the Sheraton Towers in Manhattan.
"Sometimes it's not easy doing what has to be done to talk about difficult issues, to withstand powerful forces, and that's what Carl McCall has tried to do his entire life," Mrs. Clinton said.
Senator Clinton acknowledged that Mr. McCall's chance to win was slim, but added, "You can't overlook the kind of message that Carl has, of opportunity and education and hope" and urged his supporters to continue fighting.
Mr. McCall also campaigned on Staten Island, speaking at a church with a predominantly black congregation and visiting a home for the elderly. Wrapping himself in rhetoric about overcoming obstacles and improving education, he told his supporters to ignore reports that his campaign had not caught on.
"You've heard about the polls," he said. "You've heard the pundits saying things don't look good. I don't believe that."
Mr. McCall may not have the money to broadcast his message, however. If not for $2 million in loans from his running mate, Mr. McCall's campaign for governor would have been $1.27 million in debt by the middle of this month, according to campaign finance reports released yesterday. The loans came from Dennis Mehiel, a multimillionaire businessman running for lieutenant governor.
The need to rely on Mr. Mehiel for money illustrates just how desperate Mr. McCall has become to gather the money necessary to challenge Mr. Pataki credibly. The reports released yesterday provide many examples of just how out of balance the fund-raising contest is. By Oct. 21, the last filing deadline, the governor had raised $42.8 million and spent all but $3.6 million of it, with most of the nearly $40 million going toward television commercials. Mr. McCall had raised $14.7 million -- including the $2 million in loans -- and spent $14 million, leaving him with $722,285 in the bank.
Besides the loan from Mr. Mehiel, the largest source of donations to Mr. McCall during the three weeks covered by the reports was $500,000 from the New York State Democratic Committee. Another $122,500 came from high-profile Democrats: Assembly Speaker Sheldon Silver, Attorney General Eliot Spitzer, United States Representative Charles B. Rangel, United States Senator Jon S. Corzine and Andrew M. Cuomo, his former opponent.
Aside from these politicians, only 11 other individuals gave $10,000 or more to Mr. McCall, while 38 people each gave $10,000 or more to Mr. Pataki.
Strategists on both sides say the contest has become static. McCall and Pataki aides say internal polls suggest that Mr. Pataki has a commanding lead among likely voters, with somewhere between 40 and 45 percent of the vote. Mr. McCall has been stuck between 25 and 30 percent. Mr. Golisano remains in third place with less than 20 percent in most polls, though his aides maintain he is doing better than that.
Mr. Golisano, too, has resorted to aggressive attacks on Mr. Pataki's ethics. Yesterday he charged the governor's campaign with failing to reimburse the state for the use of state aircraft for political events since 1999. Mr. Pataki said the allegation was baseless. Records show the Pataki campaign did give the state the small sum of $573 in May 2002 to cover travel for the entire year of 2001. They do not plan to pay for the governor's 2002 travel on state airplanes until next spring, aides said.
The campaign generally picks up half the cost of a plane if the governor does political events that are mixed in with run-of-the-mill announcements deemed to be state business, Pataki aides said. The governor's campaign has spent about $91,000 on chartered aircraft for campaign travel this year.
"It's not true," the governor said of Mr. Golisano's allegations. "We have made sure that we reimbursed."

HEADLINE: Draft Charter for Europe Points the Way to a Bigger World Role 

BYLINE:  By PAUL MELLER 

DATELINE: BRUSSELS, Oct. 28 

 BODY:
A draft constitution for an enlarged European Union was unveiled today, pointing the way toward creating a greater European presence on the world stage. But as it heads there, Europe has to decide on a name. 
 The draft document was prepared under the leadership of Valery Giscard d'Estaing, the former president of France. It seeks to raise Europe's profile by proposing common foreign, security and defense policies. 
 These policies are now handled by national governments. Britain and France, for example, sit on the United Nations Security Council, but the European Union is not a member of the United Nations.
Eastward enlargement of the 15-nation union was the main factor that prompted the review of the European Union's constitution. In 2004, the union is expected to take in 10 new members, largely from the former Soviet bloc to the east, almost doubling its size.
With about 445 million citizens, the European Union will have a much larger population than the United States, something the drafters of the constitution want reflected in its role in international affairs. 
The draft constitution leaves many of the questions it poses unanswered. The 15-page document is little more than a skeleton containing chapter headings.
The debate about its final shape will continue through to next summer, when a final version will be handed over to heads of state for ratification.
The draft constitution examines the role of the European Union's foreign policy chief, currently Xavier Solana, with a view to elevating the position to the level enjoyed in the United States by the secretary of state. 
It also suggests creating an elected president for the union and a Congress of the Peoples of Europe, consisting of members of both European and national parliaments. 
The expansion of the European Union will require new ways for the organization to operate internally. The draft constitution seeks to curb member states' rights to veto important union decisions.
"There'll be very few cases left" that require a unanimous vote, Mr. Giscard d'Estaing said at a news conference last week after a meeting with European heads of state. 
Then there is the question of what Europe plans to call itself. The document suggests four names: the European Union, as it is now called, the European Community, United Europe, or -- most contentious of all -- the United States of Europe. 
"This translates into nothing less than a constitution for a federal E.U. state," said Jens-Peter Bonde, a Dane who is a member of the European Parliament but who is considered a Euroskeptic. 
"There will be opposition from those who think there is too little democracy in this," he said, "and opposition from those who like me say this is the creation of a state and not cooperation among states." 

CORRECTION-DATE: October 30, 2002

 CORRECTION:
An article yesterday about a draft constitution for the European Union misspelled the given name of the union's foreign policy chief, whose position would become more powerful. He is Javier Solana, not Xavier.

HEADLINE: THE 2002 CAMPAIGN: THE HOUSE; 
Chads and Butterflies Give Way to Social Security and Prescription Drugs in Florida

BYLINE:  By DANA CANEDY 

DATELINE: FORT LAUDERDALE, Fla., Oct. 26 

 BODY:
Like so many other local Florida officials, Tom Feeney and Carol Roberts took the short and bumpy ride from obscurity to renown in the protracted 2000 presidential election.
 Mr. Feeney, speaker of the Florida House, made headlines when he threatened to have the Republican-controlled State Legislature appoint a special slate of electors for George W. Bush. Ms. Roberts, a Democratic county commissioner in Palm Beach, was a fierce supporter of Al Gore at the very center of the recount battle. 
 But this year, as they hit the campaign trail for themselves, running for Congress in different districts, they have found that the election that vaulted them to prominence is now two-year-old ancient history.
"People don't care about that," said Mr. Feeney, who has not made his role in the 2000 vote an issue in his own campaign. "People care about prescription drugs, war and the economy."
Ms. Roberts, who does not side with Mr. Feeney on much, agrees with him on this. She was recruited by Democratic officials in Washington because of her steadfast demand in 2000 that every one of the county's disputed butterfly ballots be counted by hand. But as a candidate herself, Ms. Roberts has played down her role in the election fiasco.
"I believe I defended democracy," she said, "but the issues are the issues of today."
For Ms. Roberts, who is challenging Representative Clay E. Shaw Jr. in the 22nd District, running from Palm Beach to Broward County, including Fort Lauderdale, the most pressing issue is the price of prescription drugs. Perhaps the most talked-about part of her campaign is her telephone line, 1-866-RX-CAROL, which encourages the elderly to buy prescription drugs from Canada over the Internet -- a practice she admits is illegal, though few if any patients are prosecuted for it.
"Our neighbors in Canada can buy the very same F.D.A.-approved drugs for two to three times less than we pay," Ms. Roberts tells callers in a taped message. She urges them to leave names and addresses and says, "I will send you information on purchasing drugs from Canada."
Mr. Shaw, an 11-term incumbent from Fort Lauderdale, dismissed the telephone line as an inappropriate ploy. Never mind that such drug purchases are illegal, Mr. Shaw says, "the Food and Drug Administration says it is dangerous do that."
Ms. Roberts acknowledged that the practice might not be entirely legal but said that no one was being prosecuted and that the drugs came from manufacturers approved by the Food and Drug Administration. She said her number had received more than 3,000 calls from people seeking cheaper prescriptions.
Mr. Shaw said he was seeking another term to continue working on legislation to protect Social Security and provide prescription drug assistance to the elderly -- by legal means. Beyond the prescription phone line, he said, his opponent has not distinguished herself in the race and is "just beating up on me."
Mr. Feeney, a conservative Republican with strong party ties in Washington, is running against a Democratic lawyer, Harry Jacobs, in the newly redrawn 24th District in Central Florida, which includes the Kennedy Space Center.
While Mr. Feeney has not brought up the 2000 election in this campaign, he has not been shy about promoting his ties to Republican Party insiders, including J. Dennis Hastert, the House speaker, and Tom DeLay, the majority whip. 
"There is no question that in a legislative body relationships matter in terms of committee assignments in the short and long run, and the appropriation process going forward, and with perhaps having a seat at the table when key decisions are made," said Mr. Feeney, whose platform includes property tax relief, homeland security issues and generic drug availability.
Mr. Feeney was the subject of an investigation by the Florida Commission on Ethics into whether he used his position as speaker to benefit clients of his law firm. He. said there was no merit to the accusations, which he called politically motivated, and the panel concluded this week that there was not enough evidence to support them.
Mr. Jacobs contends that Mr. Feeney has long been motivated by personal interest instead of public service. "He has crossed the line between what is right and what is wrong in representing private agencies and private clients," Mr. Jacobs said. 
With the investigation behind him, Mr. Feeney is considered tough to beat, especially by a first-time candidate with little name recognition in a heavily Republican district. 
Like Mr. Feeney, another Florida Congressional candidate, State Senator Ginny Brown-Waite, is receiving campaign help from the national Republican Party. Ms. Brown-Waite is seeking to unseat Representative Karen L. Thurman, a Democrat, in the Fifth District, in the northeast corner of the state, in a close race that is considered the most competitive in the state. Vice President Dick Cheney campaigned for her at a Tampa fund-raiser this week, and Mr. Hastert and Mr. DeLay have also lent their support.
"With a margin as close as it is, this seat is important to both parties," said Ms. Brown-Waite, whose campaign has focused on a pledge to oppose moves to privatize Social Security.
Not to be undone by the Republican Party support for her opponent, Ms. Thurman has also had a lift from her party in Washington. The Democrats tapped her this month to respond to President Bush's weekly radio address, giving her a national platform for focusing on her central campaign issue, lowering prescription drugs costs.

CORRECTION-DATE: October 31, 2002

A map on Tuesday with an article about Florida Congressional election campaigns located three districts incorrectly, and the article misstated the location of the Fifth District. The district is northwest of Orlando, not in the northeast corner of the state. A corrected map appears today, on Page A24.

HEADLINE: Technology Briefing Hardware: Dell Chief Says Hardware Demand Is Improving 

BYLINE:  Bloomberg News 

 BODY:
 Michael Dell, the chief executive of Dell Computer, said yesterday that worldwide demand for computer hardware had improved and that the Far East would be a good market for the world's biggest personal computer maker. "Globally, we've seen some rebound in overall demand," Mr. 
Dell said at a news conference in Tokyo, where he introduced the company's smallest desktop computer. "There's great opportunity for Dell, especially in Japan and the Asia Pacific." Dell surpassed the Hewlett-Packard Company in the third quarter to again become the world's biggest personal-computer maker, the market researcher IDC said earlier this month. Mr. Dell, above, reiterated that a rebound in PC's will be driven by improvement in corporate customers' profits. "We continue to face a challenging market in an economic sense around the world, but Dell is succeeding by maintaining a relentless focus on customers," he said. Shares of Dell, based in Austin, Tex. fell 10 cents yesterday, to $28.94.

HEADLINE: Arts Groups And Artists Find Angels: Universities 

BYLINE:  By KAREN W. ARENSON

 BODY:
As artists and arts organizations find themselves facing yet another brutal decline in financial support, they are increasingly turning to a small but growing corps of universities that are quietly helping fill that gap.
 The colleges are providing not just rehearsal facilities, technical support and audiences but also money for new works. 
 One of the most ambitious examples was the recent decision by Columbia University and the University of Michigan to invest $2 million in a stage production of Salman Rushdie's novel "Midnight's Children," to be performed by the Royal Shakespeare Theater at the Apollo Theater in Harlem, in Ann Arbor and in England.
In the last 15 years or so Ohio State University, the University of Iowa, the University of Michigan, the University of California and Miami-Dade Community College have poured tens of thousands of dollars into helping established artists like Mark Morris and the Joffrey Ballet as well as newcomers like Rokafella and Kwikstep, hip-hop artists from New York.
A college may contribute only $5,000 or $10,000 to sponsor a new work. But combined with four other contributions, that can add up to $25,000 to $50,000. 
Some of the money may be taken from the university arts centers' general budgets, including ticket sales and university subsidies, and some is raised from individual patrons and foundations specifically for commissioning.
No one has a firm count of how much money the universities or anyone else invests in new works each year, but experts like Sam Miller, executive director of the New England Foundation for the Arts, estimate that roughly two dozen universities are active in commissioning now. 
The University of Michigan is typical. Its University Musical Society has brought orchestras, theater companies and dancers to Ann Arbor for more than a century. But until a decade ago it did not think of itself as an originator of new works. Now it regularly commissions three or four new works a year, usually in partnership with other universities and arts centers.
"A decade ago it was satisfactory to put on a good gig and cross your fingers that you would make money," said Kenneth C. Fischer, the society's president. "Then there was a change in the climate and the sense of responsibility to do more. Now we put commissioning money in our budget, and it is a commitment each year, just like Xeroxing." He said a study about the role of performing arts organizations in the late 1980's helped change his thinking. 
Universities are not the only commissioners, of course. But with philanthropic giving and other arts revenues falling, they have come to stand out more. And foundation executives like Olga Garay, program director for the arts at the Doris Duke Charitable Foundation, argue that universities should be doing even more. "There are pockets of this kind of activity," Ms. Garay said, "but there hasn't been a field-wide acknowledgment of what these universities should aspire to."
Others, however, like Thomas Bender, a historian at New York University, express concern that the universities, too, will be forced to pull back.
"Universities have been rich enough in the past decade to embark on such things," Professor Bender said. "With a crashing economy, I worry a bit whether they will be able to do this work. And if not them, who?"
Many of the universities say they feel a sense of obligation to support the creative arts. Some see it as a means of enriching the cultural life on campus and beyond. Others believe it adds to their prestige and helps attract better faculty and students. Still others hope to make better use of handsome, well-equipped performance centers.
"When you build a new performing arts center, you want to say that art is not only about the past but about the present, too," said Leon Botstein, president of Bard College in Annandale-on-Hudson, N.Y. He said he plans to commission more work when Bard's new arts center, designed by Frank Gehry, opens next spring.
"We don't have a patronage class the way we did with Rockefeller and Carnegie," Mr. Botstein said, "and we don't have government funding like European societies do, so educational institutions have to step into the breach."
Artists who have received commissions say their work can help introduce art forms to students. "The type of work I do is a great catalyst for kids, and certainly college kids," said Elizabeth Streb, whose New York dance company, Streb, is known for the way its dancers run and crash into things. "We are not doing step dancing or pirouettes, but falling 30 feet or diving through glass walls. I think students think differently after they see us, and then go to see other dancers. We're pretty good bait."
Ms. Streb has received money or residencies or both from several universities, including the University of Illinois (the Krannert Center for the Arts gave her $25,000 for "Action Heroes"), Ohio State (the Wexner Center for the Arts commissioned "Up," her first trampoline dance, gave her a residency to work on it and also commissioned a sound score for it), the University of California at Berkeley and George Mason University.
But other artists say the efforts to involve faculty and students do not always work smoothly. Paul Dresher, a composer and artistic director of the Paul Dresher Ensemble in San Francisco, says he sometimes finds tensions between the presenters who give him commissions and the academic departments.
"It is not uncommon that I'll be on tour and the music departments won't want to have anything to do with us," he said. "It gets into the area of turf. Turf is a major part of the academic reality."
Still, he said, the university presenters, "are aesthetically committed to new work in a way that very few commercial presenters are."
One of the most active campuses has been the University of Iowa, which has commissioned or co-commissioned more than 80 works since 1986, including "Ghost Opera" by Tan Dun for the Kronos Quartet, "Geometry of Miracles" by the Canadian director Robert Lepage, and the Joffrey Ballet's "Nutcracker," for which Iowa put up $500,000, about a third of the cost. In return, Joffrey performed at the university, used dozens of local children in its performances and visited with students at the university and in the community.
One of the works Miami-Dade Community College is supporting on its $100,000-plus annual commissioning budget is a tango/opera that will have its premiere in Buenos Aires in November and probably in Miami next year. Like most of the colleges, Miami-Dade raises much of its commissioning money from foundations and other private donors and has developed a loyal audience.
At other universities, budgets are more modest. The University of California at Davis, which has a $7 million annual performing arts budget, spends about $40,000 on commissioning. It supported several new works, including a fanfare for brass ensemble and a piano quintet by faculty members for the opening of its Mondavi Center for the Performing Arts this month. And Brian McCurdy, the center's director, said he hopes to do more.
"This is a uniquely American phenomenon," he said. "There is nowhere else in the world where universities play this role, having major arts centers on campus and being major arts centers for their regions. It is certainly easier to sell tickets for Yo-Yo Ma or Itzhak Perlman, who we also present. But one of our goals is to try to develop an audience for new work."
Not all universities see commissioning of established artists as a good idea. Mary Schmidt Campbell, dean of the Tisch School of the Arts at New York University, said that Tisch preferred to focus its resources on its students.
"We think of this place as a crucible for new work by our students, and we invite directors and actors in to work with them," she said.
The interaction between American colleges and artists is not new. Lynn Garafola, a dance historian and critic at Barnard College, recalled how Martha Graham and Doris Humphrey did some of their most important work in the 1930's at Bennington College, when the college provided housing, studios and performance space during the summers.
At some universities, commissioning started through the efforts of one professor, like Roberta Uno, a theater professor at the University of Massachusetts at Amherst and longtime artistic director of its New World Theater. Thirty years ago she began commissioning new work out of frustration that there were so few works by minority playwrights to present.
At other universities commissioning has been shaped by the people who line up performances for their campus performing arts centers. At the University of Iowa, for example, it was Wallace Chappell, who was director of the university's 2,500-seat Hancher Auditorium from 1986 to 2001 (and now works for American Ballet Theater in New York). He said his previous theater experience, which included directing at the Mark Taper Forum in Los Angeles, led him "into a strong taste for the rigors and risk of new work -- 'moving the art form forward.' " 
But, he added, he had strong backing from university officials and worked to develop a constituency for new work and donors to support it.
Some of the university commissioners, like Judy Mitoma, director of the seven-year-old Center for Intercultural Performance at the University of California at Los Angeles, say they think of their work as the equivalent of science research, but in the arts.
"Just as we have research in medicine," Ms. Mitoma said, "we have research into the creative process."

HEADLINE: BP Results Fall Short And Targets Are Lowered 

BYLINE:  By SUZANNE KAPNER 

DATELINE: LONDON, Oct. 29 

 BODY:
The cold reality of a slowing economy is grinding the optimism out of BP.
 The company reported earnings for the third quarter today that fell short of expectations, and said that, for the third time in two months, it had to lower its oil production targets for 2002. 
 While rivals like Exxon Mobil and Royal Dutch/Shell have tended to set modest targets, BP had been aggressive, aiming for growth of 5.5 percent to 7 percent for the year. As recently as Oct. 2, the company said it was still hoping for 4 percent growth; now it is aiming for 3 percent, in line with its more cautious rivals. 
BP said the latest downward revision was attributable to hurricanes in the Gulf of Mexico and technical problems at operations in Alaska and Britain. Some analysts said that the earlier goals had not been realistic given the economic climate.
Lord John Browne, the chief executive of BP, said he was conducting a review to determine whether the company could meet its targets for 2004 and 2005 of 5.5 percent a year. Doing so, he said, would require investments of about $1 billion a year.
Investors reacted to the disappointing news by bidding BP's American depository receipts down 7 percent, to $36.63, its lowest level since 1998. Shares of Exxon Mobil and the British and Dutch halves of Royal Dutch/Shell also traded lower.
Until recently, BP had been known for meeting and even exceeding the ambitious targets it set, a reputation that makes the production cutbacks more alarming, analysts said.
"This is a company that had a lot of credibility attached to management, and that credibility is damaged now," said Philip Lawlor of Royal London Asset Management. "It looks as if management isn't in control of the company's affairs."
BP has suffered a series of setbacks recently, including an an explosion at a well in the Greater Prudhoe Bay area of Alaska and problems with a pipeline running from Britain to Belgium. But analysts said the company's problems were more fundamental than those temporary setbacks, and that they extended to the entire industry.
"Production at mature oil fields in Alaska and the North Sea are declining faster than new fields are coming on line," said Bruce Evers of Investec Henderson Crosthwaite.
For the third quarter, BP said its earnings, excluding one-time items and amortization costs, had fallen nearly 14 percent, to $2.29 billion, from the third quarter of 2001. Net income for the period rose 78 percent, to $2.84 billion, because of $1.77 billion in exceptional gains.
